# LPIC-1 Exam 102 (Version 5.0) Cert Prep

> Earning the LPIC-1 certification—a distribution-neutral Linux certification—shows potential employers that you have the real-world skills needed to excel as a Linux administrator. In this course, Grant McWilliams covers what you need to know to ace LPIC-1 exam 102, version 5.0. Grant reviews topics that correspond to each exam objective. Learn about shells and shell scripting; managing user and gr

#### What you should know
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For this course we'll be using CentOS 7 Enterprise Linux, which is a clone of Red Hat Enterprise Linux 7 as well as Ubuntu 19.04 running in VirtualBox virtual machines.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=11)** In this course, when I refer to Enterprise Linux 7, I mean Red Hat Enterprise Linux 7 or CentOS 7 Enterprise Linux.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=18)** Anything we do on one will work on the other.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=21)** When I refer to Ubuntu, I really mean Debian-based distributions.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=24)** This could include Debian and Linux Mint.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=27)** For this course we'll install Linux in VirtualBox virtual machines.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=31)** There are versions of VirtualBox for Windows, macOS, Solaris, and various Linux distributions.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=36)** For this course, I'll be running VirtualBox on a Linux host.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=40)** In order to run virtual machines for this course, you'll need a computer with an Intel or AMD 64-bit CPU with virtualization support.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=47)** Intel CPUs with virtualization support will be advertised as having VT-x and AMD calls it AMD-V.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=54)** You will also need a 64-bit host OS.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=58)** If you're using Linux, this is automatic.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=60)** If you're using Windows, make sure you have a 64-bit version of that OS.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=64)** If your OS is 64-bit, then you'll want to download the 64-bit version of VirtualBox as well.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=70)** For this course, you may also want to have high speed internet for downloading ISO images and doing Linux operating system software updates.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=76)** You will also want to have 32 to 50 gigabytes of free hard drive space minimum.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=81)** This will provide enough space for two CentOS 7 Linux guest VMs and one Ubuntu 19.04 guest VM.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=87)** If you have more free space available, it can give you more flexibility for creating additional guest VMs or giving your VMs larger virtual hard drives.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=96)** To run more than one VM at a time, you'll need to have at least four gigabytes of free system memory if you're Linux as your host OS.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=102)** If you're using Windows as a host OS, I recommend eight gigabytes of RAM minimum.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=107)** Notice that these hardware requirements are not that large.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/what-you-should-know?u=76281980&t=110)** Most computers built in the last 10 years will work fine.

#### About the LPIC 1 exam
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The Linux Professional Institute created the LPIC-1 certification in 1999, about 10 years after the first Linux kernal was released as an entry level, distribution agnostic certification.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=12)** In 2010, the Linux Professional Institute teamed up with CompTIA and created a combined set of exams.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=17)** This resulted in two exams, LXO-103 and LXO-104.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=24)** Exam takers received both certifications if they passed these exams.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=28)** These exams were retired in October of 2019, so we won't focus on them in this course.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=34)** The new LPIC-1 version 5 goes back to being an independent certification offered by the Linux Professional Institute, which validates skills of IT professionals.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=43)** This certification is not product based and covers tools and technologies from many different distributions including Red Hat, Debian, and SUSE.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=50)** And unlike the previous certification, it's not tied to the CompTIA Linux+ certification.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=56)** This course covers the new LPIC-1 version 5.0, 102 exam, which includes shell scripting, user interfaces, and graphical desktop environments, as well as accessibility.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=67)** For administrative tasks, it covers how to manage user and group accounts, scheduled tasks, localization, and managing system time.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=76)** It goes on to include system logging, and mail transport agents, as well as managing printers.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=82)** The 102 exam also covers general networking concepts, such as IP version 6, subnetting, and configuring Linux networks manually, as well as student network manager.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=92)** It covers networking and DNS troubleshooting and ends with host system security and encryption.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=98)** This course covers exam 102 only.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=101)** We also have a course in the course library that covers LPIC version 5.0 exam 101.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=106)** So be sure to check that out.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=108)** The LPIC-1 exam 102 has 60 questions, and you have 90 minutes to complete them.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=113)** The exam costs $200 U.S. dollars and the exam can be taken at many Pearson VUE testing centers around the world.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=120)** The certification is valid for five years.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=123)** The exam is, or will be, available in English, German, Japanese Portuguese, Chinese, Spanish, Italian and French.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-the-lpic-1-exam?u=76281980&t=131)** Not all languages are available at exam launch time.
>

#### Centos 7 Lab setup
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] For the best experience, you'll want install VitrualBox guest editions in your CentOS 7vm.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=5)** This will allow you to do fullscreen mode and have a seamless mouse experience.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=9)** First, make sure your network is turned on by going to your top right-hand menu in your rhhost1 VM.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=15)** Click on Wired Off and then click on Wired Settings.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=18)** Then click the slider button next to your wired connection.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=21)** This activates the network connection but does not make it persistent.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=25)** To make it persistent, click on the gear icon next to the slider button.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=28)** Now click on Connect Automatically and click on Apply.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=33)** Now you can close this window.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=37)** Now let's update all software packages by opening a terminal and then typing in 'sudu yum update -y' and hit enter, and then enter your password if prompted.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=50)** This process should have updated all packages including kernels.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=53)** Now reboot by typing in 'sudu reboot' and hit enter.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=57)** Do not skip this step, or installations of the guest editions will likely fail.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=63)** After your VM reboots, log in.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=66)** Once logged in, open a terminal and type in 'sudu yum group install -y "Development tool"'
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=82)** and hit Enter, and enter your password if prompted.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=87)** This installs the software development tools.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=90)** After they're done, we'll install the kernel headers.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/centos-7-lab-setup?u=76281980&t=95)** Now let's install the kernel headers.

### 1. Customize and Use the Shell Environment

#### Set environment variables
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are two types of variables accessible in a shell session.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=3)** Environmental variables are variables that are defined for the current shell and are inherited by any child processes or child shells.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=11)** Shell variables are variables that are contained exclusively within the shell in which they were defined.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=17)** They are often used to keep track of data like the current working directory.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=22)** To see the environmental variables in a terminal type in print end and hit enter.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=28)** If you want to get the value of an individual variable you can specify it.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=31)** Type in printenv.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=33)** And for instance, type in capital SHELL and hit enter.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=39)** Another way is to use the echo command to print the value by typing in echo space dollar sign capital s-h-e-l-l, and hit enter.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=48)** To see the shell variables we use the set command.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=51)** Type in set and hit enter.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=53)** By default the set command shows shell functions as well as variables which can be quite long.

> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=73)** Now type in set again and let's pipe it to less.

> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=77)** Here we can see all the shell variables without the shell functions.

> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=104)** And there it is.
>

>

> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=169)** We can see that the variable is no longer part of the environment.

>

>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=210)** We can change the value of environment and shell variables by setting them.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=214)** We can also change batch options using the set command.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=218)** However, these values only survive for the current login session.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=223)** To make them persistent, we need to add them to one of the bash startup files.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=228)** Bash stores its configuration settings in multiple startup files.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=232)** Different files are processed depending on how the shell starts.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=236)** When we log into Linux by typing in a password, Linux first executes the /etc/profile which in turn executes scripts in /etc/profile.d.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=247)** These two items are the system-wide file and directories holding our environment and shell variables.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=252)** Then bash executes the ._profile in the user's home directory which holds environment and shell variables specific to the user.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=264)** The local .bash_profile in turn executes the local .bashrc file which holds aliases and bash functions.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=273)** In CentOS the local .bashrc also executes the system wide /etc/bashrc file which holds system wide aliases and functions.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=284)** When that is complete it returns control back to the local .bashrc file in the user's home directory.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=291)** The local .bashrc file holds aliases and functions specific to the user.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=297)** Once all of this is done the user logs in.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=299)** If the user is already logged in and executes a shell script, it's executed in a shell, but it's not a login shell.
>
> **[5:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=307)** Also, if a user's logged into the GUI and starts a shell, it's not a login shell.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=313)** Generally speaking a login shell requires entering a password or providing a login key.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=319)** For non-login shells, only the user's .bashrc gets run.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=324)** Again, on CentOS the local .bashrc file executes the global /etc/bashrc file which holds system wide aliases and functions.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=334)** Then it returns control back to the local .bashrc file again and finishes processing it.
>
> **[5:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=340)** This file should contain aliases and functions specific to the user.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=345)** Once this is all done, the shell starts.
>
> **[5:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=348)** In summary the various bash profile files are for environmental and shell variables useful for interactive logins.
>
> **[5:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=358)** If you want to set a variable for all users, set it in either the /etc/profile or a script in /etc/profile.d.
>
> **[6:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=366)** If you want to set a variable specific to a user, change it in the user's .bash_profile.
>
> **[6:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=374)** If you want to add aliases or functions for all users, add them to the /etc/bashrc.
>
> **[6:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=380)** If you want to add aliases or functions specific to a user, add them to the user's .bashrc.
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=387)** It's important to note that when you add aliases or functions to the user's .bashrc file be sure to add them to the end of the file after .bashrc has been called to override system wide aliases and functions.

#### Bash functions for frequent tasks

> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=6)** These shortcuts can be in the form of a shell function or Command alias.

> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=62)** As such, use this video as a source of information on where to look for the startup scripts more than an exact reference.

> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=69)** A login shell, is a shell that we log into the system with interactively or it could be a non interactive login shell if it started with the dash dash login option.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=78)** Either way, they're still both login shells.

> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=80)** One we interact with and the other we don't.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=84)** In either case, Bash reads and executes the commands and /etc profile first, and then ~/.bash_profile ~/bash_login and lastly ~/.profile, which are all in the user's home directory and specific to that user.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=101)** Depending on the distribution, one or more of these scripts may spawn other scripts.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=105)** In the case of Enterprise Linux seven, the /etc/profile also executes all scripts in the /etc/profile.ddirectory that end in .sh, as well as the /etc/profile.d/sh.local script.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=121)** If you want to create a shell function that would be available to all users, you would want to create a file in the /etc/profile.ddirectory and name the file with a .sh ending.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=131)** After the etc profile has run, the .bash_profile in the user's home directory is read and executed.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=138)** With Enterprise Linux seven this runs the .bashrc file and the user's home directory as well.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=143)** The users .bashrc then includes the contents of the global/etc/bashrc file.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=149)** It does this by sourcing the file.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=151)** Sourcing a file runs the contents of the file in the current shell.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=154)** The dot command is a synonym of the source command.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=158)** Sourcing a script is different than running it because it includes the commands in the current environment like they were in the current script as opposed to spawning a new shell with a new environment.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-functions-for-frequent-tasks?u=76281980&t=167)** For other distributions, this process may be carried out differently.

#### Bash aliases as shortcuts
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] An easy way to create a shortcut to a command is by using an alias.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=4)** Shell aliases get executed before anything else, so you can override other commands with them.

> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=9)** Let's take a look at the Bash execution order.

> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=20)** And lastly, the commands on-disk.

>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=51)** An alias creates a shortcut for any command string.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=55)** To exemplify how to create an alias, we will create a simple one that will speed up how we navigate the file system.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=61)** Normally, to change into a parent directory, we type in cd ..
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=65)** Normally, to change into parent directory, we type in cd ..
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=69)** We could shorten this to ..
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=71)** To do so, type in alias ..="cd .."
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=80)** Now, when we type in .. it should execute cd, space.. , making navigating the directory structure faster.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=87)** Note that this is a pretty simple alias that may make our workflow faster, but I often use aliases for creating shortcuts for really long command strings.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=95)** For instance, r is sinking an entire directory to a remote server using specific options.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=100)** This is not something I want to remember every time so I created an alias for it.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=104)** Now let's save and exit this file by pressing escape + colon + x + exclamation mark and hitting Enter.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=112)** Because the .bash RC script gets sourced only when we start a shell, these functions and aliases will not be active yet.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=118)** We can either close our shell or source the file.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=121)** Let's source it, type in source ~/.bashrc and hit enter.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=128)** Now let's try our alias, type in .. and hit enter.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=132)** We can see that this moved us up to the parent directory.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=135)** If we have an alias with the same name as a command, and we don't want to execute the alias, we can prefix the command with the backslash.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=142)** Let's look at our aliases again.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=144)** Type in clear and then type an alias, and hit enter.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=148)** We have an alias named ls to force the non aliased ls to run type in /ls and hit enter.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=155)** This will run the non aliased ls and will display the listing without color.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=160)** However, this doesn't guarantee that a command will run as there may be a function or built in named ls as well.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=166)** If we want to force the execution of a command on disk, we can prefix it with the word command.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=171)** For instance, type in command ls and hit enter.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=175)** This will run a command on the disk named ls not a function or alias.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=180)** If you don't know if something is a command or alias, use the type command.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=184)** Enter type - a ls and hit enter.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/bash-aliases-as-shortcuts?u=76281980&t=188)** Here we can see we have an alias named ls and two on-disk commands.

#### Set command search path
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Bash has some pre-specified locations to look for commands in.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=4)** These locations are stored in a variable called path.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=7)** When you type in a command, Bash will look for the command in each of the directories in the path variable and execute the first one it finds.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=14)** This means if you have the same command in more than one directory, the one that it finds first will get executed.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=19)** We can look at the path variable using the echo command.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=22)** In the terminal type in echo space dollar sign capital PATH and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=27)** Each directory is delimited by a colon and Bash looks from the left to the right.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=32)** This variable will have different contents depending on the user and the users can add directories to their copy as well.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=38)** If you want to search for command in the path without using the more complex find or locate commands, just use the which command.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=44)** Type in which space and then the command.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=46)** In this case I'll use tar and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=49)** This will tell you which directory tar is in if it's in your path.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=53)** If you want to get more data including the man pages for the command use the whereis command type in whereis space tar and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=62)** To add a directory to the path variable you need to put it in one of your shell startup scripts.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=66)** Let's create a directory called scripts and add it to our path variable.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=69)** Type in clear, and then type in mkdir space scripts and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=75)** Now it's added to our dot Bash RC file.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=77)** Type in vi space tilde slash dot bash RC, and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=83)** Go into insert mode by pressing the I key and go down to the bottom and we're going to add two lines.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=88)** Type in capital PATH equals dollar sign capital PATH colon tilde slash scripts.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=96)** Then hit Enter and type in export space capital PATH.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=101)** Note that we're assigning the path variable with the current contents of the path variable plus our scripts directory.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=107)** Also note that we didn't include the dollar sign when exporting the variable.

> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=111)** Now Save and Exit by pressing escape X exclamation mark and hitting Enter.

>

> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=123)** Type in source space tilde slash dot bash RC and hit Enter.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=129)** This will process the file immediately.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=131)** Let's take the path variable again.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=133)** Type in echo space dollar sign capital PATH and hit Enter.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=137)** And we can see our scripts directory at the end, you will definitely want to log out and back in to make sure it works on a new shell as well.

> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-command-search-path?u=76281980&t=144)** If you're an administrator and you want to add a directory to every users path variable, you'll need to do it at a system level.

#### Maintain skeleton directories

#### Introduction to Bash
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=0)** - Bash is the Default shell on Enterprise Linux, and as such, is the default vehicle for Shell Scripts.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=6)** As of this recording, the version of Bash that comes with Enterprise Linux seven, is four dot two.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=11)** Older versions of Linux may target an older version of Bash, or even call the lowest common denominator, The Bourne Shell.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=18)** Every Shell script specifies the shell it wants to be executed with.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=21)** Although this can be overridden on the command line.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=24)** Bash as a version four dot two, has the following features.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=27)** If then else if conditionals, Case statements, Conditional Tests for, file ownership, file permissions, numeric equivalence, text equivalence, file type is a file, file type is a directory, file type is a block device or character device, file is readable, writable or executable, a file exists or a directory exists.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=54)** For Loops, C-Style For Loops, While Until Loops with conditions.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=60)** Brace Expansion, Arithmetic Expansion, Command Substitution, Integer Math, Mathematical Conditionals, Indexed Arrays, Associative Arrays, Extended Globs, and Expanded Regular Expressions.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=78)** Other features include; functions, substring operations, parameter substitution, increment and decrement operators, debug traps, positional arguments, sub shells, and lastly co-processes.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=93)** Bash is good for automating tasks, and interacting with the operating system.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=97)** There are jobs like running multiple processes asynchronously, and waiting for all processes to finish before moving on, that are very simple in Bash, but complex and other languages.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=107)** There are times when you shouldn't use Bash, for instance, Bash does not handle complex data types like XML or JSON, nor can it handle floating point math internally, and relies on external commands for this.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=118)** Bash, if written poorly, is difficult to troubleshoot, as it's loosely typed and often runs code, even if it doesn't work.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/introduction-to-bash?u=76281980&t=125)** Also, there's no object orientation in Bash.

#### What is a shell script?

#### Execute compound commands
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] A simple command is any of the commands you type in on a regular basis.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=3)** For instance, ls or grep root /etc/psswd.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=10)** These are simple commands.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=12)** When we refer to compound commands, things get more complex.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=16)** A compound command, is a couple of commands that are grouped in some way and are all executed in close succession.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=22)** We have a couple of ways of doing this, we can specify a second command that runs after the completion of our first command, type clear and then type in echo "hi" ; echo "there" and hit enter.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=40)** This will run the first echo command and when it completes, it runs the second echo command.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=46)** Note that the second echo command runs no matter where the first command succeeds or not.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=49)** This is really the same thing as typing in the first command, hitting enter and typing in the second command and hitting enter again.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=56)** It just queues up the command so we don't have to wait.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=58)** If you only want the second command to run, if the first command succeeds, you can use a double ampersand symbols.

> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=215)** Let's redirect our first compound command example to a file type in echo space double quote, Hi, double quote space semicolon space echo space double quote there.

> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=226)** double quote space greater than Hello dot txt and hit enter.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=233)** Note that hi ended up on the screen.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=235)** Now let's view the text file.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=236)** Type in cat space Hello dot txt and hit enter Only the words there is in the file, only the second echo got redirected to the text file.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=245)** Now let's use curly braces, bring your line back and add in the left curly brace.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=251)** And then after there, add in a semicolon and a right curly brace, and then hit enter again.
>
> **[4:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=257)** Now we'll view the txt file again, type in cat space Hello dot txt and hit enter.
>
> **[4:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=261)** We can see that both lines are in this file, the curly braces force bash to process all commands inside the braces as one command.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=269)** This would allow us to pipe or redirect the output.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=272)** Now let's do the same thing but replace the curly braces with parenthesis, bring your line back again and change the curly braces to a left parentheses and a right parentheses and hit Enter again. And once again we'll view the text file we get the same thing as using curly braces.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=292)** The difference between curly braces and parentheses is that the latter runs the commands on a sub shell.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=297)** The curly braces run the commands in the same shell.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=300)** Since the commands are running in a sub shell, they'll have different variable scope.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=303)** To show this type in clear.
>
> **[5:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=305)** Then we'll type in a couple of different commands, a equals zero semicolon.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=310)** And for our second group, left parentheses, a equals 10, semi colon space echo space double quote, I N equals dollars sign a double quote, right parentheses, semi colon.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=324)** For our last group, echo space, double quote, out, equals dollar sign a double quote.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=333)** In the first command a is assigned to zero, then inside the sub shell is assigned to 10.
>
> **[5:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=338)** Then we echo inside the sub shell, and again outside the sub shell, hit enter.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=343)** You can see from the output that these are actually two variables named a one in the main shell and one in the sub shell and they hold different values.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=350)** If you want to have separate variable space, use parentheses for a sub show.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/execute-compound-commands?u=76281980&t=354)** If you just want to group commands that execute as one thing use the curly braces

#### Conditional flow with if, then if
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=0)** - [ Instructor] Conditionals in Bash are fairly similar to other languages.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=3)** The syntax looks like this.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=6)** It can be shortened a bit by placing the then on the same line as the if, separated by a semicolon.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=13)** If we want to include an else section, we can.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=16)** We can also check for more than one condition.

>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=22)** This can be slow for a lot of conditions.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=25)** The condition can be a test of some sort, or it can be the successful execution of a command.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=31)** This is one thing that separates Bash from other languages.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=34)** Bash is very tightly integrated with the operating system.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=37)** In this example, we search etc password for the word root using grep.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=41)** If it's found in grep, will return a zero to the if conditional and the first code block will run.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=47)** If grep doesn't find root, then it will return a non-zero number and the second code block will execute.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=52)** We can negate this result as well.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=54)** To run the first code block if the command doesn't succeed, use an exclamation point before the command.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=60)** This is good for error messages.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=61)** For instance, we could attempt to copy a file, if it fails, then output a message on the screen.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=67)** We will cover the actual test we can do as an if conditional in another video.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=71)** But let's talk for a moment about single square brackets versus double square brackets.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=76)** Single square brackets are POSIX compliant.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=78)** They work with older shells including Bourne, and are commands that then test the condition.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=84)** The first single square bracket is really the built in test command.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=87)** The second square bracket is an argument to the first.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=90)** You can use the /usr/bin/test command and get the same results.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=95)** With single square brackets, file name expansion and word splitting happening.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=99)** This may not be what you want.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=101)** Parameter expansion also happens.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=104)** Ampersand ampersand, pipe pipe, less than and greater name operators, get interpreted by the shell, since a square bracket test isn't a statement but rather a command.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=113)** The shell interprets these special characters for its own use.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=116)** Lastly, quoting is required to preserve variable values.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=121)** The double square brackets are not commands but rather built into the Bash shell as keywords.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=125)** They're not POSIX compliant, and are specific to bash and ksh.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=129)** They do not work with older shells.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=131)** No filename expansion happens between brackets, this may be good.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=136)** No word splitting happens between brackets, this is usually good.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=140)** Parameter expansion does happen between brackets as well as command substitution.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=145)** They support ampersand ampersand, pipe pipe, less than and greater name operators.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=149)** These operators are used for Boolean logic to do more complex tests as well as text order comparisons.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=156)** They also support automatic arithmetic evaluation of octal and hexadecimal.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=161)** In addition, they support extended regular expression matches.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=164)** This is really the killer function.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=166)** Regex conditions bring a great deal of power to bash conditionals.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=170)** Lastly, quoting is not required.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=172)** Although I still quote out of habit, you can get away with not quoting with double square brackets as the area between the brackets is protected.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=180)** I recommend in almost every case to use double square brackets.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=183)** There are more weird conditions where single brackets break than we have time to go into here.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-if-then-if?u=76281980&t=187)** Double brackets are more reliable, and consistent, and are the future in most cases.

#### Conditional flow with case
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Generally, we use if conditionals and Bash.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=3)** However, in cases where we're matching more than one pattern with an if, then else-if conditional block it may be more efficient to use a case statement.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=11)** The case statement evaluates the condition one time and acts accordingly.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=16)** This statement evaluates the age variable.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=20)** If the value is one through nine numerically, then it executes the Action List to the right of the parentheses until the double semicolons which is the Action List Terminator.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=28)** The characters to the left of the parenthesis make up a glob.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=31)** You can do pattern matching in the same manner as you would on the command line using wildcards, character sets, and character classes.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=37)** However, you cannot use regular expressions which is unfortunate.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=41)** The equivalent if conditional would evaluate age up to four times to do the same thing so this case statement would be much faster.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=48)** The last condition which is asterisk catches everything that doesn't match above.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=52)** This would be similar to the else portion of an if then else conditional block.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=57)** Also, the last condition doesn't need to be terminated by double semicolons as a statement ends anyway.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=62)** The default behavior of a case statement with double semicolons as Action List Terminators is to execute only the list associated with the first matching pattern and then the case statement ends.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=72)** For example, if AGE=5 then both the first and second patterns would match but only the first Action List would get executed because the case would end up to the first match.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=82)** However, in Bash 4, there are two new Action List Terminators double semicolon Ampersand and semicolon Ampersand I've changed the example to reflect this.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=92)** The double semicolon Ampersand Action List Terminator is similar to double semicolons but instead of exiting after the first magic continues to process matches.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=101)** If it finds a second pattern that match it executes that Action List as well.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=106)** Now, if AGE=5, the first pattern would match and the Action List would get executed since the Action List Terminator is double semicolon Ampersand then the case statement would continue on and match against the second pattern and the second Action List would get executed as well.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=122)** This semicolon Ampersand Action List Terminator automatically executes the next list without evaluating its pattern.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/conditional-flow-with-case?u=76281980&t=128)** So if age was between 10 and 19, the third pattern would match and the Action List for that match would run but because we have the semicolon Ampersand Action List Terminator the next Action List would also be executed.

#### Iterate with for loops
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Whenever you want to loop through a finite list of items, you may want to use a for loop.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=5)** The syntax for the for loop looks like this.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=8)** We start with a list of items to loop through, as we iterate through them, they're assigned to a variable.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=13)** In this example, the variables name is item.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=15)** Inside the loop, we do something with the contents of the variable.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=19)** The list for the for loop can come from any number of sources.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=22)** It can be a static list that we create such as one, two, three, four, five, or a list of names.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=28)** If your looking for a list of sequential numbers, we can create a dynamically.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=32)** In the past, we'd use a sequence command for this.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=35)** And we'd use command substitution to get it to run.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=38)** However, it's better to have Bash create the list using expansion.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=42)** This way we're not spawning a new shell and executing commands.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=45)** It's more reliable and it's faster.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=48)** There are times though when we will use command substitution to create the list.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=52)** However, be very careful because the for loop uses the IFS or internal field separator to word split.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=59)** The IFS by default splits on blank spaces so if a feel name has a space in it, your loop will break.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=65)** You can change the IFS temporarily while you're looping.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=68)** When you're done, you will want to reset the IFS to what it was before or other commands will break.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=75)** This does work, but is a bit messy.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=77)** Perhaps a better option will be to use while read which we'll talk about in a later video.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=83)** Another possibility is to loop through the values of an array.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=87)** Note that we're not putting quotes around the list.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=89)** This is because quoting it will result in there only being one item in the list.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=94)** In this example, we're looping through items in an indexed array.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=98)** Since the array in indexed, it would be better to loop through the index instead.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=103)** Let me explain what this does.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=105)** First we create the array.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=107)** Then we get the number of array items using the hash array left square bracket at right square bracket syntax.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=114)** Now we do math, do subtract one since the array count started from one and we will be looping starting from zero.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=122)** Next we use a sequence command to create our list from to number of array items minus one.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=127)** Now each iteration, that index number's assigned to the variable named 'i' and we use that to access the array item specifically.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=135)** Earlier I mentioned that we don't use a sequence command anymore because Bash has parameter expansion.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=140)** You may be wondering why we can't this example a lot simpler by using parameter expansion.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=146)** The problem is that parameter expansion happens before math and variable expansion.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=150)** Our only choice is to run a sub shell with the sequence command.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-for-loops?u=76281980&t=154)** It is possible to get tricky with the eval command to have Bash create the sequence, but then we defeat the purpose of trying to make it simple.
