#### Iterate with while loops
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The for loop is designed to loop through a finite number of items and when it gets to the end, it exits.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=6)** It is possible to have a condition inside the for loop that will break out of the loop even before it runs out of items.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=11)** A better idea is to use a loop with a condition built in.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=15)** For this we have the while loop and until loops.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=17)** The only difference is that the while loop will iterate while a condition is true and the until loop will iterate until the condition is true.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=24)** I usually use a while loop over the until loop.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=27)** Let's take a look at the syntax of the while loop.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=29)** The condition inside the single square brackets follows the same rules as an if conditional inside of single square brackets, which means you can match on wildcards, but not regular expressions.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=39)** The simplest while loop we can make is the infinite loop by replacing the condition with the word true.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=44)** This loop will keep iterating forever, unless the user kills the script or the condition inside of the if condition double square brackets is true and the break command runs.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=52)** By nesting an if conditional inside of a while true loop, we can take advantage of the if conditional's double square brackets in regular expressions.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=61)** Let's take a look at a while loop with a condition.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=63)** The variable I is set to zero, the loop continues as long as the value of I is less than four.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=69)** Each time the loop iterates the value of I is increased.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=72)** Once it reaches four, the loop exits.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=75)** One major advantage of using a while read loop over a for loop is that it handles different types of text better.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=80)** This is the example that we used earlier in this course.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/iterate-with-while-loops?u=76281980&t=82)** Any text file provided on the command line will be read by the while read loop and it will handle blank spaces just fine.

#### Using return codes
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-return-codes?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-return-codes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Every Bash command or function returns a code when run through signal to the OS whether the process was successful or not.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-return-codes?u=76281980&t=7)** If the operation succeeds, it will return a zero return code.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-return-codes?u=76281980&t=10)** If it fails, it will return a nonzero value from one to 255.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-return-codes?u=76281980&t=15)** The reason that zero is success and one through 255 are failures is that our process can fail for many reasons.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-return-codes?u=76281980&t=21)** Using many numbers for failures allows Bash to give more granular output for troubleshooting reasons.

> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=107)** We're doing this because we need it to match the format that the log file uses as it will be part of our search criteria.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=112)** On line 15 we search the log file for authentication failures.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=117)** The line has to start with a date and can be followed by any number of characters, but must include the words authentication failure.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=123)** We're doing a case insensitive search and counting the number of results.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=128)** On line 17 we do a similar search, but add EUID equals zero to the end.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=133)** This will show a root login failures.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=135)** Lines 19 through 21 add lines of text to the report including the date, the total number of failed logins, as well as the number of failed root logins.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=143)** Lines 23 through 25 check to see how many failed root logins happened during the day.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=149)** If it's higher than the threshold, it emails the report to the admin user.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=153)** The last line emails the report to the other email address no matter how many results there were.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=159)** This is not a perfect script, as I've tried to keep it short, but I'll leave it up to you to modify it and make it better.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=165)** This same type of script could monitor anything.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=167)** For instance, we could monitor how much space a user's home directory is taking up, their network data usage, their CPU usuage, or even monitor the firewall for certain types of packets.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-a-monitor-script?u=76281980&t=176)** It could be adapted for many different situations.

### 3. Install and Configure X11

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### X11 architecture
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=0)** - UNIC systems historically have used the X Windowing System to provide the constructs for graphical interface.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=6)** X supports screens, drawing and moving of windows, as well as input devices such as mice and keyboards.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=13)** The X Windowing System was created by MIT in 1984.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=17)** Version 11 of the protocol was released in 1987 and has been at that version ever since.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=23)** A free version of X was released in 1991, called XFree86.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=28)** In 2004, XFree86 distributed code that conflicted with a GPL license, so it was forked into [X.org](https://X.org).
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=35)** Most Linux distributions switched to [X.org](https://X.org), and the last version of XFree86 was released in 2008.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=41)** The development of XFree86 has stopped.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=45)** When I talk about X in this video, assume I'm talking about the X Windowing System, XFree86, or [X.org](https://X.org).
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=52)** The architecture for all is the same.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=55)** X Windows is network-aware by default.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=58)** A service called the X server exists where the video card and input hardware is.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=62)** This means the X server is usually on the local work station.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=65)** It handles input from the keyboard and mouse and outputs to the video card.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=70)** The graphical applications that run are X clients.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=73)** By default, the X clients talk through the local network with the X server, using the X protocol.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=79)** Because the architecture is built from a client-server model, it's easy to have remote graphical applications.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=84)** But where the client and server is in this situation is the opposite of what you might think.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=89)** If I run an application such as Firefox on a remote host, the graphical interface comes back to me on the local machine, but the application is running on the remote CPU.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=99)** Remember that the server is where the input and video hardware is, so the X server is local to me, and the client application is remote.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=106)** This is the opposite of most client-server relationships.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=110)** X has some problems, though.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=112)** It must remain backwards compatible, so the system has gotten quite complex in the last 40 years with multiple different rendering engines, driver systems, and blobs of code all over the place.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=122)** As such, it's hard to make fast.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=124)** Also, our needs have changed.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=126)** 3D applications, such as games, have proven more difficult with X than with other systems.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=131)** X was great for servers, but as desktop computers have gotten more common, we've needed a new system.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=137)** The leading replacement for X is Wayland.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=140)** Development for Wayland started in 2010.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=142)** The first release was in 2012.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=145)** Adoption has been slower than expected, but most distributions offer Wayland now.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=150)** Wayland differs from X in that the architecture is simpler.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=153)** There's no monolithic server in the middle.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=155)** It doesn't handle drawing.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=156)** It supports direct rendering by the client.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=159)** Memory management, command scheduling and mode setting are in the kernel.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=163)** Other functions are pushed into libraries, leaving Wayland much smaller and lighter.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=167)** For backwards compatibility, Wayland supports having an X server as a client.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/x11-architecture?u=76281980&t=171)** And lastly, Wayland doesn't currently have networking ability, although it's been worked on.

#### About desktop environments
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] With Linus, the graphical interface is just a set of applications running on the OS.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=5)** This means we can change out the graphical interface and use a different one if we wish.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=9)** On Linux a system called X Windows provides the ability to have a GUI including mouse pointer, keyboard and screens.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=16)** On top of X-Windows will be a GUI Toolkit.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=19)** Usually GTK or QT.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=22)** The window manager and desktop environment use the toolkit to create the windows, launchers, toolbars and menus.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=28)** It's also important to note that all applications work on all desktops.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=33)** Choosing one desktop doesn't exclude using applications from another.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=37)** However, applications for KDE might not look native on GNOME desktop and vice versa.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=43)** There are many reasons why different desktop environments exist.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=46)** Perhaps one desktop environment uses less memory or another is designed for a touch interface.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=52)** It's important for us to find the desktop environment that fits our needs.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=56)** Desktops may also include extra applications including word processors, spreadsheets, application launchers, configuration tools, and more.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=66)** In this course, we will be using CentOS 7 Linux.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=69)** The only officially supported desktop are GNOME 3 and KDE 4.10.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=74)** Even though only two desktops are officially supported, we can install other desktop environments by adding third party software repositories.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=82)** Let's begin by talking about GNOME 3, as its the default desktop on CentOS 7.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=87)** GNOME 3 is part of the GNU Project, is stable, and has a fairly unique way of working.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=92)** Overview mode was introduced with GNOME 3 and is a paradigm shift in workflow.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=97)** GNOME doesn't even include an application menu by default, and instead relies on going to overview mode, and typing in the application's name.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=106)** It does have an icon dock, for starting common applications.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=110)** GNOME applications use the GDK3 windowing tool kit.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=114)** The other officially supported desktop is KDE Plasma Workspaces, which is a more Windows like desktop environment with a traditional application menu.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=123)** Some call KDE the most powerful desktop on Linux, and for multiple reasons.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=128)** It has a semantics search solution and activities.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=131)** Activities allow a user to switch between configurations quickly.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=136)** KDE uses the QT toolkit, spelled Q-T, setting KDE applications a part from most other desktops.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=143)** The next few desktop environments are not officially supported in CentOS, and as such we need to add third party software repositories to install them.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=152)** MATE may be the most popular third party desktop for CentOS 7.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=156)** This is because it is essentially GNOME 2.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=160)** Some users didn't like the drastic changes made in GNOME 3, so they forked the old code and renamed it MATE.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=166)** MATE is a simpler desktop with a traditional workflow.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=170)** MATE uses the GTK2 toolkit.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=173)** One of the problems with MATE is that it uses the older GTK2 libraries.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=178)** Some developers were concerned that these libraries will become obsolete so they set out to recreate GNOME 2 using newer GTK3 libraries from GNOME 3.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=188)** The name of this new desktop is Cinnamon.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=190)** It was created by the Linux Mint Distribution to provide a modern alternative to GNOME 3 but with a more traditional workflow including an application dock and application menu.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=201)** The last desktop environment we'll look at is Xfce.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=206)** Xfce has a traditional workflow with an application menu.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-desktop-environments?u=76281980&t=211)** It's main claim to fame is that it uses less memory and CPU cycles than the other desktops that have more features.

#### Assistive technologies
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/assistive-technologies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/assistive-technologies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Most modern operating systems, including Linux, support assistive technologies for users with hearing, speech, vision, or mobility impairments.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/assistive-technologies?u=76281980&t=8)** Assistive technologies include applications for keyboard navigation, on-screen visual keyboards, screen readers, speech synthesizers, as well as visual and audio alerts.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/assistive-technologies?u=76281980&t=18)** For those wanting easier to read and or higher contrast text we can change to a higher contrast view of the desktop, and or one with larger text.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/assistive-technologies?u=76281980&t=25)** To do so, go to applications, system tools, and then settings.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/assistive-technologies?u=76281980&t=30)** Now click on universal access.
>

#### Override specific aspects of Xorg configuration
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the past with x386 we had to configure X Windows manually by editing the configuration file.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=6)** When Xorg for was released it's ability to auto-configure the graphical interface and input settings improved and, in many cases, we no longer have a configuration file at all.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=15)** However, we can still manually configure it if you wish, by creating a /etc/x11/xorg.conf file.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=24)** There are multiple configuration sections in this file to handle different settings, different sections are then tied together to represent one complete configuration of devices.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=33)** The file section is used to specify path names required by the server.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=38)** The ServerFlags action is used to specify some global Xorg server options.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=42)** The module section is used to specify which Xorg server module should be loaded.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=47)** This section is ignored when the Xorg server is built in static form.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=51)** The type of modules normally loaded in this section are Xorg server extension modules.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=57)** The extension section, is used to specify which X11 protocol extensions should be enabled or disabled.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=64)** The config file may have multiple InputDevice sections.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=67)** Recent acts serve as employee HAL or udev back ins for input device enumeration and input hot plugging.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=75)** If hot plugging is enabled, input device sections using mouse, keyboard, and VMMouse drivers will be ignored.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=82)** If hot plugging is disabled we'd manually configure our mice and keyboards here.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=88)** The config file may have multiple input class sections.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=90)** The sections are used to provide configurations for entire classes of input devices as opposed to setting a specific device.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=98)** We can match on products, vendors, drivers, layouts, et cetera.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=102)** The config file may have multiple device sections.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=105)** There must be at least one for the video card being used.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=108)** The identifier entry for the section specifies a unique name for this graphic device.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=114)** The driver's section specifies the name of the driver to use.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=117)** A device section is considered active if it is referenced by an active screen section which we'll get to in a moment.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=124)** The config file may have multiple monitor sections, which describe the capabilities of our video monitors including resolution and scan frequencies.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=133)** The config file may have multiple mode sections.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=136)** These sections provide a way of defining sets of video modes independently of the monitor sections.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=143)** The config file may have multiple screen sections there must be at least one for the screen being used.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=148)** A screen represents the binding of a graphics device and a monitor.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=153)** A screen section is considered active if it's referenced by an active ServerLayout section.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=159)** A ServerLayout represents the binding of one or more screens, and one or more input devices to form a complete configuration.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=167)** To create the ServerLayout we'd have a device with an identifier and driver.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=171)** We'll combine the device with a monitor to form a screen.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=174)** We'll also have an input device which specifies a mouse and keyboard.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=178)** We'll then create a complete configuration by combining the input device with a screen into the ServerLayout.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=184)** Although we can create an entire Xorg configuration file manually, it's not really recommended.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=190)** Any section that we leave out of the config will be auto-configured by Xorg.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=194)** A better practice, is to override parts of the config by placing pieces of the configuration in /etc/x11/xorg.conf.d Let's go to that directory and list it.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=207)** In a terminal type in cd/etc/ upper case X11/xorg.conf.d/ and hit enter.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=215)** Type in LS. We see a filename 00-keyboard.conf.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=220)** Let's take a look at it with cat, type in cat space 00-keyboard.conf and hit enter.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=227)** We see that as defining an input class and specifying the keyboard layout as "US" we could override this by editing this file.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/override-specific-aspects-of-xorg-configuration?u=76281980&t=235)** We can override any of the Xorg settings using this same method.

#### About remote desktop protocols
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Linux operating systems are designed with remote access built in.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=4)** Most often that is via the SSH command line.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=7)** However, there are protocols to allow remote GUI access, as well.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=11)** X Windows is the de facto standard for remote desktop on Unix and Linux systems plus there is VNC, or Virtual Network Computing, RDP, or Remote Desktop Protocol, a protocol created by Microsoft and a new protocol named SPICE.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=25)** In most cases remote GUI access is accomplished by using an application on the client which connects to a service on the Remote Host via a remote desktop protocol.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=34)** The Remote Host updates the display on the Local Host.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=37)** The Local Host then sends keyboard or mouse events back across the network to the Remote Host.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=42)** The Remote Host updates the Framebuffer based on the results of the mouse and keyboard actions.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=47)** X Windows, the legacy GUI system used by Linux, supports remote desktop by default.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=52)** X Windows is the de facto standard for graphical desktops on Linux although Wayland is replacing it.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=59)** With X Windows is a client which resides where the application is running and a server where the video card and input devices are.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=66)** However, it only works when X Windows is installed on both a client and server which in most cases is a Linux or Unix client connecting to a Linux or Unix server.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=75)** In a local, non-remote environment, the client and server are on the same host.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=80)** The X Client communicates with the X Server to display its desktop and handle input devices.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=86)** In a networked environment the Client moves to the Remote Host and the server resides on the local one where the video card and input devices are.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=93)** This may seem opposite of what you'd expect as servers are usually remote to us.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=98)** The X Protocol is fairly efficient.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=101)** Because X supports drawing objects, not just bitmap data it supports objects such as screens, windows, pointers and mouse and keyboard input.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=110)** It supports object-based screen updates.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=112)** It can do whole remote desktops or just export single applications.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=117)** For security it can be tunneled through SSH easily.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=121)** NX, a third-party extension, provides extra features that improve X including automatic encryption provided by SSH, bitmap compression, X Windows messages compression, session management and local data caching.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=135)** The end result is greatly improved speed and usability and X does not, however, support 3D desktops like GNOME 3 or Unity which calls into question its future in Linux.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=146)** For systems that don't support X Windows let's take a closer look at other remote desktop protocols such as VNC, RDP and SPICE.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=154)** VNC, or Virtual Network Computing, was created at the Olivetti & Oracle Research Lab.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=159)** In 2002 it was bought by AT&T and the lab was shut down.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=163)** The developers left and started RealVNC.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=166)** VNC uses the concept of a remote Frame Buffer.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=170)** It transmits the keyboard or mouse events from one computer to another relaying the graphic screen updates back in the other direction over the network.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=177)** It essentially transmits a photo of the screen.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=180)** As such, it's simple to implement from a programming standpoint.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=184)** The default RFB protocol is not secure and transmits passwords in plain text.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=189)** Also the base protocol isn't very fast and is fairly bandwidth heavy.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=193)** Other versions of VNC have been released to address some of these issues like UltraVNC and the commercial version of RealVNC which support encryption.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=203)** TightVNC recommends using SSH or VPN to tunnel all traffic instead of including encryption.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=209)** TightVNC also includes extensions to compress the graphics data lessening bandwidth load and improving speed.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=216)** VNC is platform-dependent.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=217)** There are clients and servers for many GUI-based operating systems and for Java.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=222)** Popular uses for this technology include remote technical support and accessing files remotely.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=227)** With VNC each connection will be to a different VNC server port.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=231)** If we had two users who want access to the desktops remotely we need to start two servers on the Remote Host running on two different network ports, one for each user.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=240)** RDP, or Remote Desktop Protocol, is a proprietary protocol developed by Microsoft for doing remote desktop.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=247)** RDP Version 10 supports 32-bit color space, 128-bit encryption, audio and file system redirection, printer, serial, and parallel port redirection, USB port redirection, shared clipboard between the local and remote systems, seamless windows in that you can send a window across instead of the entire desktop, multiple monitor support and virtualized GPU support.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=272)** RDP clients exist for many different operating systems besides Windows including Linux, Unix, macOS, iOS, Android and others.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=281)** RDP used to be called Terminal Services for Windows and is now called Remote Desktop Connection.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=288)** RDP servers exist for Windows operating systems, Unix, macOS.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=293)** There's also an open-source RDP server called xrdp for open-source operating systems.
>
> **[4:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=299)** SPICE is the Simple Protocol for Independent Computing Environments.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=303)** SPICE is a remote desktop display system built for virtual environments which allows users to view a computing desktop environment remotely.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=311)** In 2007 Qumranet developed SPICE as a closed-source protocol.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=315)** In 2008 Red Hat purchased Qumranet and open-sourced it.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=319)** It supports TLS tunnels for security and various types of authentication including RSA and SASL which enables other OS-supported types like Kerberos, is used in the Qemu and KVM virtualization stack.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-remote-desktop-protocols?u=76281980&t=333)** And there's XSPICE for standalone remote desktop situations.

> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/display-applications-on-remote-x-servers?u=76281980&t=192)** And zero is the screen number.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/display-applications-on-remote-x-servers?u=76281980&t=194)** Now run Gooey application by typing in its name.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/display-applications-on-remote-x-servers?u=76281980&t=197)** For instance gnome dash system dash monitor, and hit enter.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/display-applications-on-remote-x-servers?u=76281980&t=203)** The gnome system monitor is running on rhhost2, but the Gooey is on rhhost, this would allow us the crhhost2 system resource usage graphically.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/display-applications-on-remote-x-servers?u=76281980&t=213)** With a bit more work, it's possible to bring the entire desktop back to rhhost1.

> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/add-and-removing-users?u=76281980&t=4)** User accounts are stored in /etc/password, passwords and account aging are stored in /etc/shadow, and other account defaults are stored in /etc/login.defs and /etc/default/useradd.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/add-and-removing-users?u=76281980&t=17)** This last file also specifies our skeleton directory.

#### Modify users

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] In order to change a user's account settings after they've been created, we'll need to use the user mod command.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=7)** Most of the SIN text for user mod is identical to that of user add.

> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=15)** Dash D lets you change the user's home directory.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=19)** We might use this if we manually move their home directory and need to update etsy password.

> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=24)** Dash G and dash U options allow us to change the primary group ID as well as the user's user ID.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=32)** Dash capital G is for adding users to supplemental groups.

>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=43)** Dash A and combination with dash capital G allows you to append people to supplemental groups.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=49)** Dash L allows us to change the user's login name.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=53)** Dash capital L and dash capital U allow us to lock and unlock users' accounts.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=60)** User mod also has the ability to move a user's home directory using the dash M option.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=66)** And lastly, dash S allows us to specify the default login shelf or the user.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=72)** User mod has a few more options for expiring accounts and setting the inactive period on accounts.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=78)** However, I used a change command for these things.

> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=81)** You can watch the video in this course on individual user account settings for more information.

>

> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=157)** Type in cat space slash E-T-C slash group and hit enter.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=162)** We'll need to scroll up to the audio group and ensure that Sally's name is in the last column.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=167)** We can also lock and unlock accounts using user mod.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=171)** Type in sudo space usermod dash capital L space Sally and hit enter.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=179)** We can verify that her account is locked by viewing the etsy shadow file again.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=183)** Type in sudo space cat space slash E-T-C slash shadow and hit enter.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=192)** We'll notice that the very first character of her encoded password is now an exclamation point.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=197)** Let's go ahead unlock her account by typing sudo space usermod space dash capital U space Sally and hit enter.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=206)** Now verify that the exclamation point is gone by viewing etsy shadow again.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=211)** Type in sudo space cat space slash E-T-C slash shadow and hit enter.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=219)** Then we can see that the exclamation point is now gone.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=223)** The last thing we'll do is restrict the Sally user so she can't login.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=227)** If we use the user mod dash capital L option, it just locks the password.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=232)** If Sally was logging in through as a sage, and had working S-S-H keys, should could still login.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=238)** To keep her from logging in at all we can change her shell to slash sbin slash no login.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=244)** Type in sudo space usermod space dash S space slash sbin slash no login space Sally.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=255)** What we're doing here is we're changing her shell to slash sbin slash no login.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=260)** We can verify this by catting etsy password.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=263)** Type in cat space slash E-T-C slash P-A-S-S-W-D and hit enter.
>
> **[4:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=270)** If we look at the last column, we'll see that her shell is slash sbin slash no login.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=276)** To reverse the process, just change it back to slash bin slash bash.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=280)** By typing in sudo space usermod dash S space slash bin slash bash space Sally and hit enter.

> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/modify-users?u=76281980&t=289)** For more information on the user mod command you might want to check out the man page by typing in man space usermod and hitting enter.

#### Manage groups
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Groups are an important part of the Linux User Management system.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=4)** However, groups have one major limitation, they can't be nested, so you can't have a group within a group.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=10)** To create a group we use groupadd.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=13)** Groupadd has a few options, but the most common is dash g for specifying the group ID.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=19)** Let's create a group called accounting with the group ID of 1050.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=23)** In a terminal type in sudo space groupadd space dash g space 1050 space accounting and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=33)** Type in your password and hit Enter again.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=36)** We can verify this by viewing the Etsy group file.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=39)** Type in cat space slash etc slash group and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=44)** We see the last line has our accounting group and the group ID is 1050.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=50)** If later we want to modify attributes of our group, we can with groupmod.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=55)** For instance, if we want to change the group ID, we would type in sudo space groupmod space dash g space 1051 space accounting.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=67)** This will change the group ID of the accounting group to 1051.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=73)** If accounting were the primary group for any users, their accounts would be automatically updated to reflect the changed group ID.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=80)** To place users in our new group we can use a g password command.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=85)** My user name is grant, so to place my user in the accounting group, I would type, sudo space gpasswd space dash a space grant space accounting, and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=102)** Specifying a user for our group is a very group-centric way of thinking about the problem.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=107)** We can also be more user-centric and modify the user so they belong to a group.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=113)** To do this, we would type in sudo space usermod space dash a space dash capital G space accounting space grant, and hit Enter.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=126)** Remember that if you want to add the accounting group to a user's current list of groups, you have to include the dash lowercase a option.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=135)** To delete a group, we'll use the groupdel command.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=138)** Type in sudo space groupdel space accounting, and hit Enter.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-groups?u=76281980&t=145)** Verify that the group has gone by viewing Etsy group, type in cat space slash etc slash group and hit Enter.

#### Manage special purpose and limited accounts
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Most accounts in Linux are the same in that the user accounts with user IDs higher than 500 or 1000 depending on how the system was set up.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=8)** You can see this configuration setting by viewing the /etc/login.defs file.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=12)** In the terminal, type in cat /etc/login.defs and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=19)** On CentOS, the starting UID for regular users is 1000.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=23)** This is common across most current distributions.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=25)** All user accounts with IDs less than 1000 are system accounts and often, they're not login accounts, meaning the users can run processes and own files, but they cannot log in to a shell.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=35)** We can see this by looking at the /etc/password file.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=38)** Type in cat /etc/password and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=42)** If we look at the last column for each user, we can see which ones are login users because the shell will be /bin/bash or another login shell.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=50)** If the account is not a login user, it will have /sbin/nologin or /bin/false.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=56)** We could also have login users that can only run one custom application by placing that application in the last column using user mod.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=64)** When the user logs in, that application will run automatically.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=68)** Keep in mind that it's more complex than it seems as the /etc Linux security policy needs to be modified to allow custom applications to run.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=75)** There are also third-party restricted shells that can be installed and used such as rsh, rbash, bash with a --restrcted option, rksh, and even ksh -r.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=88)** However, they are not foolproof and we won't be covering them here.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=92)** For elevated privileges, we have a special account in many Linux distributions named wheel.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=97)** It itself doesn't give us special privileges, but it's often listed as the group that admins are in.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=102)** Let's take a look at the /etc/security/access.conf file using less.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=107)** Type in less /etc/security/access.conf and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=115)** Now search for wheel by pressing the forward slash and typing wheel.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=119)** Here we see some special privileges for anyone in the wheel group, including the ability to shut the machine down.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=125)** Non-local logins to the wheel account are also restricted.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=128)** Press Q to quit.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=130)** Let's also look at the sudo configuration.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=132)** Type in sudo vIsudo and hit Enter, and type in your password if prompted.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=137)** Once again, search for wheel, press your forward slash, and type in wheel.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=142)** We can see that users in the wheel group can run all commands with privilege escalation.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manage-special-purpose-and-limited-accounts?u=76281980&t=146)** Below this, we can see how we'd set up special sudo privileges for other users and groups as well.

#### Awareness of shadow passwords
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In most systems passwords are encoded using a one way hash such as MD5 or SHA.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=7)** A hash function takes a string of characters of any length as input and produces an encoded string of characters a fixed length, which acts as a kind of signature for the data provided.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=17)** One way hashes are not reversible and as such, you cannot get the plain text from the hash.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=23)** Note that for these slides, I've used an MD5 hash due to its shorter length.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=27)** Most modern Linux systems use a much longer and more secure SHA512 hash even though a one way hash cannot be reversed, it can be cracked by taking words from a dictionary one at a time, hashing them and comparing the output to the hashed password to see if they match.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=44)** Linux has additional protection against dictionary attacks called a salt.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=49)** A salt is a set of random characters that are appended to the password before it's hashed.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=53)** The salt is stored in the password files with the hash.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=56)** A hacker who doesn't have access to the salt, wouldn't be able to gas the correct password.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=61)** The Portion of the hashed password to the left of the dollar sign is the salt.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=66)** The portion to the right of the dollar sign is the hash.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=69)** By using a different, random salt for each password, it makes dictionary attacks all but impossible.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=75)** In order for a hacker to attempt to crack the passwords on Linux, they need to have read access to the salts and hashed passwords.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=82)** In a system that doesn't use a shadow suite, the encoded user and group passwords are stored in /etc/password and /etc/group.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=91)** Both of these files are world readable.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=92)** Meaning every user in the system can read them and possibly try to crack the hash passwords.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=98)** The shadow suite removes encoded passwords from etc password and etc group by placing them in etc shadow and etc gshadow, neither of the shadow files are readable by users and as such are not easy to crack.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=111)** Note that root is the super user.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=112)** So root can read both of these files.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=115)** Most Linux distributions these days have the shadow suite installed by default.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-shadow-passwords?u=76281980&t=120)** If it's not installed, we can install it by opening a terminal by going to applications, favorites, terminal.
>
