#### Change default password policies
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It's not uncommon for systems to get hacked due to weak passwords.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=5)** Administrators can tighten up password policies to require a minimum number of characters and character classes.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=11)** Linux has a credit system that affects the minimum allowed characters for a password.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=16)** Credits are given if a user uses characters from different classes like lowercase, upper case, digits, and other characters.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=24)** If our minimum password length is nine, and we include a lowercase letter, then Linux will allow an eight character password such as tqbfjotl.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=35)** If our minimum password length is nine and we include at least one lower case and one uppercase letter, then Linux will allow a seven character password, such as Tqbfjot.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=48)** If our minimum password length is nine, and we include at least one lowercase letter, one uppercase letter, and one digit, then it will accept a password that's six characters long, such as Tq8fjo.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=64)** The password policy configuration contains settings for unique characters, minimum characters, digits, uppercase characters, lowercase, and other, which includes punctuation.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=77)** It also has facilities for the minimum number of different character classes to require repeatable characters and whether to check the comment field of the /etc/password file for similarities to users' passwords.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=91)** Difok is the number of characters that must be unique in the new password.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=96)** Minlen is the minimum length for the new password.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=100)** Note that credits will subtract from this number.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=103)** If you're using credits, you will want to add one to your desired minimum.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=107)** Dcredit is the maximum credit for using digits.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=111)** A password can be one character shorter if the user includes a digit.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=115)** If you want to require a certain number of digits, place a negative number here, such as -1.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=122)** Ucredit is the maximum credit for using uppercase characters.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=126)** Use a negative number here to require uppercase characters.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=130)** Lcredit is the maximum credit for using lowercase characters.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=134)** Use a negative number here to require lowercase characters.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=138)** Ocredit is the maximum credit for using other characters, such as punctuation.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=143)** Using a negative number here will also require are other characters.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=148)** A -1 would require one other character and a -2 would require two, and so on.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=153)** Minclass defines the number of different character classes required.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=158)** You can require characters from all four classes by placing a four here.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=162)** Maxrepeat allows us to restrict the number of identical consecutive characters.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=167)** Disable this check by setting it to zero.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=170)** If you want to keep a user from having multiple digits or letters next to each other, we can set maxclassrepeat.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=178)** Disable this check by setting it to zero.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/change-default-password-policies?u=76281980&t=181)** Change gecoscheck to one if you want to check for words from the comment field in the /etc/password file longer than three characters in the user's password.

>

#### Individual user account settings
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In another video of this course we saw how to change global user account settings by editing the /Etsy/login.deskfile.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=7)** This only affects new users created after the change.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=11)** If we need to change existing user accounts we have to use various other commands.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=16)** You could edit the /Etsy/shadowfile for most of these settings, but it's not recommended as it's easy to misconfigure a user this way.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=24)** The main command we'll use to change user account settings, is chage, or change age.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=31)** Chage is used to change password and account aging information for users.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=36)** Chage -d, changes the number of days since January 1st, 1970, when the password was last changed.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=43)** Setting this to zero means the password hasn't been changed and forces the password change on next login.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=50)** - E, sets the date that the user's account will expire.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=54)** - I, sets the number of days of inactivity after password expiration, before the account is automatically locked.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=63)** - m, sets the minimum number of days before a password changes.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=68)** Setting this to zero allows users to change their password at any time.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=74)** - M, is the maximum number of days during which a password is valid.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=79)** - W, is the number of days of warning before a password change is required.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=84)** And lastly, -l, lists account aging information.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=89)** This is the only command option that can be run by any user.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=93)** Let's go to the terminal now and modify some account aging information.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=98)** Now let's get the account information by first typing in, cat /etc/passwd.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=106)** Now I only have one account with a user ID of 1000 or higher, which means a regular user account.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=112)** My username is Grant.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=114)** Let's get the account aging information for this user.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=118)** Chage -l username, which in my case, is Grant.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=121)** which in my case is grant here.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=124)** Here we can see a listing of this account's aging information.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=127)** If we want to force a user to enter their password at the next login, we can use the password command to expire their account.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=135)** Type in, sudo passwd --expire , and then the user account name.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=144)** In my case, Grant.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=146)** Type in your password again, and hit Enter.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=149)** Another way to expire a password is to use the chage command.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=152)** Type in, sudo chage -d 0 , and then the user account which in my case, is Grant, and hit Enter.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=163)** We can verify this by typing in, chage -l , and then user name and hitting Enter.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=169)** You'll notice that my password must be changed.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=172)** So before going further, I'm going to go ahead and log out by clicking on my top right hand menu, clicking on my name, and then clicking on Log Out.
>
> **[3:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=180)** Then I'll click on Log Out again.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=183)** I'm going to try to log in normally with my old password, and you'll see that it's going to prompt me to change my password.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=191)** I need to type in my old password again, and then I'll type in my new password, which I'm setting right here.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=199)** Type it in a second time, and hit Enter.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=203)** Once I'm logged in, I'll open up another terminal by going to Applications, Favorites, Terminal, make it full-screen and bump my font size.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=211)** We're going to verify my aging information by typing in, chage -l , and then my username, which is Grant.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=219)** You can see that now, my last password change was January 16, 2017.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=224)** You may also notice that we have both account expiration and password expiration dates.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=230)** When the password expires the user needs to reset their password.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=234)** The user may still be able to log in using SSH keys, but when the account expires, it can no longer be logged in at all.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=241)** Password expiration is to create a moving target for hackers.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=245)** If users have to change their password every so often it makes it harder to exploit.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=249)** Account expiration is to close accounts.
>
> **[4:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=252)** First, I'm going to change the account expiration date for the user.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=256)** Let's type in, sudo chage -E , and then I'm going to put a date in there, of 2017-11-10 , and then my username, which is Grant, and hit Enter.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=272)** I'll need to type in my new password again for sudo.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=276)** Chage -l , and my username, which is Grant.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=281)** We can now see that the account expires on November 10th, 2017.
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=286)** Now I'm going to change the password expiration to 90 days using the -M option.
>
> **[4:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=292)** Type in, sudo chage -M 90 , and then the username, which in my case, is Grant, and verify by typing in chage -l , username, Enter.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=309)** Note that this changes the password expiration date and the maximum number of days between password changes.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=315)** Typically, if the password is expired, users are forced to change it during the next login.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=320)** You can configure it so that when a user doesn't log in for a specific amount of time, after a password has expired, the account will be automatically locked.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=330)** To change the number of days the account can be inactive before it's locked to 10, we'll use the -I option.
>
> **[5:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=337)** Type in, sudo chage -I 10 , and the the username.
>
> **[5:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=344)** Hit Enter.
>
> **[5:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=345)** We'll verify this by typing in chage -l , the username.
>
> **[5:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=351)** Now that we've done all of this we may want to disable all password aging.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=354)** To do so, type in, sudo chage -I -1 -m 0 -M 99999 -E -1 the username,
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=378)** which in my case, is Grant, and hit Enter.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=381)** The -I -1 will set the password inactive to never.
>
> **[6:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=386)** The -M 0, will set the minimum number of days between password changes to zero, so the user can change their password at any time.
>
> **[6:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=394)** The -M 99999, will set the maximum number of days between password changes to 99999, or roughly 274 years.
>
> **[6:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=407)** The -E -1, will set account expires to never.
>
> **[6:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=413)** This will disable the password expiration of a user.
>
> **[6:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/individual-user-account-settings?u=76281980&t=416)** We can verify this by typing in, chage -l , and the username, and hit Enter.

### 5. Automate System Administration Tasks by Scheduling Jobs

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Reccuring user jobs using cron
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Systemd manages system services in CentOS 7.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=4)** It also manages a lot of other objects like devices, system timers, and targets.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=9)** The systemd equivalent to run levels.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=11)** Systemd objects are called units and for each unit there's a unit file for configuration.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=17)** For this course, we're only concerned with service units and service unit files.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=22)** The command that systemd uses to manage these units is systemctl.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=27)** To look at our service unit files type into a terminal, systemctl list-unit-files -at service
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=40)** and hit enter.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=41)** By default, list-unit-files -t service will show all enabled service unit files.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=48)** Enabled, meaning services that are configured to startup automatically.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=52)** By adding -a, it will show both enabled and disabled service unit files.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=58)** Notice that the systemctl subcommand is list-unit-files.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=62)** This means we're just looking at the unit files and their status.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=66)** We're not yet looking at the running status of services.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=70)** The output will be in two columns.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=72)** The first column is the service name.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=75)** The second column is the service state which can be either enabled, disabled, or static.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=81)** Enabled means it will start automatically at boot.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=84)** Disabled means it will not start automatically.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=87)** Static means that service is not enabled and has no provisions to be enabled.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=92)** Some services are not meant to start automatically.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=96)** Press Q to quit.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=98)** Let's get more information about enabled services.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=101)** Type in systemctl list-units -at service and hit enter.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=110)** By default, list-units -t service shows enabled running services.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=117)** By including the -a, it will also show enabled non-running services.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=122)** If you disable and stop a service, it will no longer show up here.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=128)** The output has five columns.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=130)** The first column is the service name.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=133)** The second column shows if the unit file was loaded.

> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=136)** The third column, or active column, shows the general state of the service.

> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=141)** The fourth column, or subcolumn, shows us more detailed information about its state including whether it's running or not.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=149)** And the last column shows the description.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=151)** Press Q to quit.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=153)** We can filter by the state of the service.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=155)** For instance, if we wanted to only list services that were active and currently running, we can type in, systemctl list-units -t service --state -running

> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=173)** and hit enter.

> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=177)** In this case, rsyslog.service.

>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=187)** I'm going to choose the rsyslog.service.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=190)** Type in clear and then type in, systemctl cat rsyslog and hit enter.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=199)** Notice the service name was rsyslog.service but the command worked without the extension.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=205)** What we see here is the unit file, which determines the service dependencies, what command it executes, and what the service should do if the command fails.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=215)** We can also get the status of a single service, as well.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=219)** Type in clear again and hit enter.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=222)** Type in, systemctl status rsyslog and hit enter.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/reccuring-user-jobs-using-cron?u=76281980&t=228)** This gives us the status of the service, when it started, what the process ID number is, and some log messages.

#### Systemd timer units
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Systemd allows you to define timer units.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=3)** Systemd timers are a powerful alternative to cron jobs.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=7)** There are two different types of timer units: real-time timers and monotonic timers.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=12)** Real-time timers activate on calendar events similar to cron jobs.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=16)** There started based on a date and time value.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=19)** We can set a timer to run every year on December 31st at midnight that says, "Happy New Year," or we could set a timer to run every Sunday that does a full backup of our system.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=28)** Monotonic timers activate after a time span relative to a starting point.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=32)** For instance, if a timer is to be activated five minutes after the boot process or 30 seconds after we log in, we'd used a monotonic timer.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=42)** The main benefits of using timers over cron jobs comes from each job having its own service file.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=47)** Also, jobs can be started independently of their timer making them easier to debug.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=52)** Jobs can be configured to run in their own environment.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=55)** Jobs can be attached to cgroups.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=58)** Jobs can have dependencies on other systemd units, such as network.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=61)** We can have job that only runs if the network is active.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=64)** And jobs are logged in the systemd journal.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=68)** Cron jobs, however, are easier to create as they're often one-liners, and have built-in support for emailing based on job failure.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=74)** This functionality however can be simulated with systemd timers.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=78)** Having units are named with a .timer extension and have a matching .service file.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=83)** We could have a backup.timer and a backup.service where the timer activates the service.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=89)** This service then could run a script of any name, such as backup.sh.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=94)** We can list our timers using the systemctl command.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=96)** In a terminal, type in systemctl list-timers and hit enter.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=103)** The output shows when the timer last ran and how long before it runs again.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=106)** It also tells the timer unit name and the service unit that it activates.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=111)** An example service file would look like this.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=113)** This time would be named backup.service.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=116)** This service runs the /root/bin/sysbackup.sh script.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=121)** This would be something we created to do the work.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=123)** We could also have an application here.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=125)** The timer that activates this service would look like this.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=128)** The type of timer is OnCalendar, which is set to activate everyday at 2:00 a.m.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=134)** This means it's a real-time calendar.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=136)** The timer unit that it activates is backup.service.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=140)** We can see that we can specify dates, in addition to times, using the OnCalendar keyword.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=144)** Using OnCalendar, we're creating a real-time calendar.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=148)** We have other keywords that we can use besides OnCalendar that will allow us to create monotonic timers.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=154)** Remember, monotonic timers are specific to a starting point.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=158)** OnActiveSec is relative to the moment the timer's activated.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=162)** OnBootSec is relative to when the machine booted up.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=165)** OnStartupSec is relative to when systemd was started.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=169)** OnUnitActiveSec is relative to when the unit the timer is activating was last activated.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=176)** This references the service unit.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=177)** This allows us to start counting from when the service unit started.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=181)** OnUnitInactiveSec is relative to when the unit the timer is activating was last de-activated.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=188)** This references the service unit again.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=190)** This allows us to start counting from when the service unit stopped.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=194)** RandomizedDelaySec allows us to specify a random amount of time in which it should activate the service.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=200)** This allows us to spread the load out.
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=203)** This has been a problem with cron jobs in the past.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=206)** A monotonic timer would look like this.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=209)** This monotonic timer uses the OnBootSec keyboard and specifies 15 minutes.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/systemd-timer-units?u=76281980&t=214)** It also specifies weekly, so it only runs once a week.

> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=232)** Date can also take the number of seconds and output a date in whatever format we want.

> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=237)** As an example, let's take the number of seconds in last operation and find the date for it.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=242)** Type in date, space, dash dash date, equals, and then inside single quotes, the @ symbol, and the number that was the result of the command previous.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=256)** I'll paste that in there, another single quote, and hit Enter, and it returns a date back.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=262)** This comes in very handy if you ever want to calculate dates.
>
> **[4:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=265)** For instance, let's say it's February 26th and we want to calculate 10 days in the future.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=271)** You could type in date, space, dash dash date, space, inside single quotes, plus 10 days, another single quote and hit Enter.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=282)** And it'll calculate 10 days for you.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=284)** It also accepts more abstract wording, date, dash dash date, single quote, next Thursday, single quote, and hit Enter.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=297)** There's a lot more that the date command can do so check out the man page for more options.
>
> **[5:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=302)** There's one more time and date tool I'd like to show.
>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=306)** Cal, hit Enter.
>
> **[5:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=308)** Cal shows you a calendar.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=310)** It seems silly at first, but it's actually quite useful.
>
> **[5:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=313)** If you wanted to show three months, we can type in cal dash three and hit Enter, and it shows us three months.
>
> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=320)** Note that this is different than typing in cal space three, which is the year number three.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=326)** You can show the calendar for any year.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=328)** For instance, cal 1752 and hit Enter.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=334)** Notice anything strange about September.
>
> **[5:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-locale-settings-and-environment-variables?u=76281980&t=336)** I'll leave that up to the viewer to research it.

#### Install and start chrony
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The Chrony server and client should already be installed by default but to be sure open a terminal and type sudo space yum space install space dash Y space chrony and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=13)** Type in your password if prompted.

> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=17)** This will not hurt anything if it's already installed.

> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=20)** After making sure Chrony is installed we need to make sure the service is running.

> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=25)** To check the status, let's use system CTL.

>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=38)** This shows our Chronyd server status.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=42)** If yours is not running you'll want to start it by typing in sudo space systemctl space start space chronyd and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=52)** And to make it start automatically at boot, we'd enable it.
>

>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=63)** and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=65)** Again this won't hurt anything if it's already running and persistent, now Chronyd will start when the system boots up.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=73)** The Chrony package includes the Chronyd ntp time server.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=76)** Chronyd by default only accepts time queries from the local client.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=82)** The chronyd server can serve up time information to clients as well as allow a client to connect to it to control it, those are configurable independently.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=91)** The chronyd server by default allows connections using local Unix domain sockets.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=96)** It can optionally be configured to allow connections using IP version four or IP version six.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=102)** The chronyd server which we've already discussed and a command line client called chronyc.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=108)** Chronyc can be used to query the local time server and also can be configured to query other remote servers if needed.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/install-and-start-chrony?u=76281980&t=115)** If our chronyd server is configured properly, we can also use chronyc to control our time server remotely.

#### Configure chrony server
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To configure our Chrony server to accept local network requests, we'll need to know our network IP address.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=6)** Make sure you're logged into your rhhost1 VM and then in a terminal, type in ifconfig.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=13)** You can also use ip space addr as well, but I find the output is easier to read on the legacy ifconfig command.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=20)** I can see that my IP address is 192.168.1.69 and my network mask is 255.255.255.0.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=31)** This makes my network address 192.168.1.0.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=36)** We need to remember this for later.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=38)** For this exercise, we'll configure chronyd to accept network time requests from clients in the local network.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=44)** For this, we'll configure chronyd, and then open a hole in the firewall.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=47)** To configure Chrony as an NTP time server, we'll want to edit the /etc/chrony.conf file.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=54)** Let's do this with Vim.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=56)** Type in clear and then type in sudo space vim space slash etc slash chrony dot conf and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=65)** Type in your password if prompted.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=69)** Comments start with a hash, percent, or exclamation point.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=72)** The first non-commented lines may be the server lines or pool lines.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=77)** If the keyword is pool, then the line specifies a pool of NTP servers as a time source.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=82)** This pool name will resolve to multiple servers, which may change over time.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=87)** At startup, chronyd will get a list of sources from the pool.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=92)** If a source is unavailable, it will attempt to replace it with another.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=96)** In my case, I don't have any pool lines, but I have multiple server lines.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=100)** The server keyword here specifies a specific server instead of a pool.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=104)** Using the server keyword, we could direct all NTP client requests on the local host to a specific time server.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=111)** The next line is the location of the drift file.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=114)** This file stores the rate in which the system clock gains or loses in relation to real time.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=119)** This is stored to allow Chrony to adjust a clock before a reliable source is established.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=124)** You should not have to do anything with this configuration line.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=128)** The next line starts with makestep and determines if Chrony can step the time if the time was too far off to adjust via speeding up or slowing down the clock frequency.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=136)** My line says make step space one dot zero space three.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=142)** This would allow stepping the clock if the adjustment is more than one second off, but only in the first three clock adjustments.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=150)** Now let's jump down to the min sources line.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=152)** This is the number of NTP sources needed before adjusting the local clock.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=156)** The default is one, but if we want to make our local time more reliable, we may require more than one source.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=162)** These sources would have to agree before a local clock is adjusted.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=167)** The allow line is what turns our Chrony installation into a time server for other hosts on the network.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=172)** It's commented out by default.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=174)** Let's uncomment this line and change it to our local network address.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=178)** For mine, I'll make it allow space 192.168.1.0/24,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=188)** as my local network address is 192.168.1.0 and my subnet mask is 255.255.255.0, or 24 bits.
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=199)** We can also allow chronyc to control chronyd by using the cmdallow keyword in the same manner, which we will not cover in this course.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=208)** The local line allows us to use Chrony to serve up time to our local network without being connected to a time server.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=215)** This is useful if we have an isolated network.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=218)** If we wanted to specify a pure host to synchronize time to, we'd use the peer keyword followed by the IP address of the peer.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=225)** We would also add the peer keyword to the other host configuration file pointing back to this host so both hosts would synchronize with each other.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=234)** This is an RHCE exam item.
>
> **[3:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=237)** Note that we can specify a server, a pool, a peer, and even local in one file.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=242)** Chrony will fall back to peer and local if the remote time sources aren't available.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=248)** To save and exit, press Escape colon X exclamation point and then hit Enter.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=256)** Now we need to allow external NTP requests to our service by opening a port in the firewall.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=260)** Type in sudo space firewall dash cmd space dash dash permanent space dash dash zone equals public space dash dash add dash service space ntp and hit Enter.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=280)** Type in your password if prompted.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=284)** In most installations, the public zone is default, so you wouldn't have to add it here.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=288)** However, if we had a firewalld zone for our internal network and one for our public network and we only wanted to allow queries on the internal network, we'd add the service to that zone only.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=300)** To enable the changes we've made to the firewall, let's reload the firewall configuration.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=304)** Type in sudo space firewall dash cmd space dash dash reload and hit Enter.
>
> **[5:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=310)** And then to verify our service has been added to the zone, type in sudo space firewall dash cmd space dash dash list dash all and hit Enter.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=321)** Here we can see that NTP is allowed in our public zone.
>
> **[5:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=324)** Now restart the Chrony service.
>
> **[5:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=326)** Type in sudo space systemctl space restart space chronyd and hit Enter.
>
> **[5:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-chrony-server?u=76281980&t=334)** Your time server should be ready to serve up time information to clients now.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** hit (6), next (2)
> **CLI Commands:** sudo (5), make (4), find (1), systemctl (1)
> **Env Vars:** ntp (6), rhce (1)

#### Use cryonyc
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we have a time server and client configured, let's use the chronyc command.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=5)** In my rhhost1 VM, I'm going to type into a terminal sudo space chronyc space tracking and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=13)** Type in your password if prompted.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=16)** The reference ID is the computer in which Chrony is currently synchronized to.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=20)** The reference time is when the time was last processed from the source.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=25)** The system time may be different from the actual time.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=27)** Chrony doesn't change the time, but rather speeds up or slows down the system clock until the two are synchronized, so there's no jump in time.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=34)** The frequency is the amount the system clock would be wrong if Chrony wasn't correcting it.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=39)** Let's check the Chrony sources by typing in clear and then type in sudo space chronyc space sources space dash v and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=49)** The dash v option provides more caption information.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=52)** The sources subcommand shows information about current time sources.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=57)** The leftmost character under the capital M is the source mode.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=61)** It will be a caret if the source is a server, an equal sign if it's a peer, and a hash symbol if it's the local clock.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=68)** The second character shows the state of the source.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=70)** It will be an asterisk if it's synchronized.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=73)** The rest of the columns provide valuable source information.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=76)** Refer to the verbose output on the screen for more information.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=80)** If you want drift and offset information for sources, use the sourcestats subcommand.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=85)** Type in clear and then type in sudo space chronyc space sourcestats and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=93)** If the cmdallow option is provided in the time service chrony.conf file, we can allow chronyc to control our time server.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=100)** Note that any configuration change through chronyc is not persistent.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-cryonyc?u=76281980&t=104)** As such, we will not be covering the cmdallow option in this course.

#### Using ntpd for serving time
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=0)** - The default NTP server for CentOS is ChronyD, however there are times where you may find yourself administering NTPD.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=7)** Let's install NDBD and use some of the tools that come with it.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=11)** First, let's stop ChronyD if it's running and disable it.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=14)** I'm logged in as user1 but you can use any user you wish, as long as it is in the wheel group so you have sudo admin privileges.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=21)** Type in 'sudo systemctl stop chronyd' and hit enter, and then to keep it from starting automatically bring your line back and change stop to disable and hit enter again.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=35)** Now let's install NTBD, type into terminal 'sudo yum install -y ntp' and hit enter.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=46)** Once that is done type in 'clear'.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=48)** The next step is to allow NTP traffic through the firewall.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=50)** Type in 'sudo firewall-cmd --permanent --add-service=ntp'
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=62)** and hit enter.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=63)** Now we need to reload the firewall rules, type in 'sudo firewall-cmd --reload' and hit enter.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=71)** At this point we have the NTP server installed, it's running, and the NTP traffic is allowed through the firewall.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=77)** So let's look at the configuration.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=79)** Type in 'sudo vi /etc/ntp.conf' and hit enter.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=86)** We can see a lot of the same settings in the ntp.conf file that are in the chrony.conf file.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=90)** For instance, we can see a reference to the drift file at the top.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=94)** If you want to allow hosts on your network to synchronize time with this server you need to loosen up the restrict lanes.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=99)** For instance if my local network was 192.168.2.0 I'd modify the restrict by un-commenting it, and changing the network to 192.168.2.0.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=112)** The nomodify and notrap statements at the end ensure that your clients are not allowed to configure the server, or be used as peers for time sync.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=120)** Now go down one line to where it says 'Use public servers from the pool.[ntp.org](https://ntp.org) project.'.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=126)** A lot of services use NTP to synchronize the system clock with a trusted external source.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=131)** This source can be an atomic clock, a GPS receiver, or another server that already uses NTP.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=137)** The NTP pool project is a worldwide cluster of time servers providing easy access to reliable time.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=143)** You can go to www.[ntppool.org](https://ntppool.org) with a web browser and choose NTP pool servers near you and then modify the lines in this config if you wish.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=153)** The included settings however, are fine.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=155)** If you want to query any of these NTP pool servers we can use the NTP date command with the '-qu' options followed by the NTP server address.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=164)** To do so, open another terminal tab and then type in 'ntpdate -qu 0.centos.pool.[ntp.org](https://ntp.org)',
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=175)** that was one of the servers in the file, and hit enter.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=179)** From this output, we can see some information about the NTP pool servers, close this tab and go back to your first terminal.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=187)** To save this file press ESC+':x!' and hit enter.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=191)** Now let's start the service, type in clear then type in 'sudo systemctl start ntpd.service'.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=202)** If you wanted to make it persistent we would bring our line back and change start to enable so it will start on reboot.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=208)** Now let's test our ntp server by querying it, type in 'ntpq -p' and hit enter.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=216)** The remote column shows the hostname of the ntp servers, the refid column shows the source of the servers.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=223)** For Stratum 1 servers it should be GPS, PPS, acdesk, or PTB.
>
> **[3:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=228)** In my case it is PPS or a generic polls per second server.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=232)** For Stratum 2 and higher it will show the IP address of the server.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=236)** The Stratum level is in the third column, following this is the poll delay and jitter numbers, the lower the value the better.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/using-ntpd-for-serving-time?u=76281980&t=243)** Now we have a working NTPD server set up, which we can practice using NTPQ and NTPD with.

### 7. System Logging

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### Locate and interpret system log files
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - Log files, are files that contain messages about the system.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=4)** These messages would pertain to the kernel services or applications.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=8)** There are different logs for different tasks.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=11)** For instance, there's a log that keeps track of failed logins.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=14)** There's another log, Just for cron jobs and so on.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=17)** Most system D based operating systems have two login systems Rsyslog log and journald.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=23)** Rsyslog is compatible sysklogd and handles persistent logs.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=27)** Rsyslog either logs to text files in the local machine or it logs across the network to a remote logging server using either TCP or UDP protocols.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=37)** Journald however, is part of system dean is not persistent by default, so the journal logs don't survive a reboot.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=43)** Since the journal is binary and it's only stored in memory, it's very fast to write to and very fast for us to search, to ensure Rsyslog is running.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/locate-and-interpret-system-log-files?u=76281980&t=51)** We can use systemctl, hyphen sudo space systemctl space start space rsyslog and hit enter.
>
