#### Read the system journal
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/read-the-system-journal?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/read-the-system-journal?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] The second method of logging data in Enterprise Linux is a service called journald which is part of systemd.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/read-the-system-journal?u=76281980&t=6)** The journal is a binary file stored in slash var slash run which is a virtual file system in RAM.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/read-the-system-journal?u=76281980&t=12)** Everything in this file system disappears on reboot, thus the journald by default is not persistent.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/read-the-system-journal?u=76281980&t=18)** Because it's in RAM, it also has a fixed size based on the amount of memory the system has.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/read-the-system-journal?u=76281980&t=22)** The data in the journal is structured and indexed so it can be accessed very quickly.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/read-the-system-journal?u=76281980&t=27)** If persistence is necessary, Journald can be made persistent, or even configured to forward journal data to our syslog.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/read-the-system-journal?u=76281980&t=33)** This gives us the ability to even send journal data to a remote log server.

#### Configuration of logrotate
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In a previous video we looked at the Rsyslog configuration file and learned about rules made up of filters and actions.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=6)** The filter determines which items in a log file to act upon.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=9)** It is made up of a facility and a priority.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=12)** An action is what Rsyslog will do.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=15)** The facility is the subsystem that produces the system message.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=19)** Facilities can be one of: kern, user, mail, daemon, auth, syslog, lpr, news, uucp, cron, authpriv, ftp, and local0 - local7.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=36)** The second half of the filter is the priority.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=38)** Priorities can be one of: Debug, Info, Notice, Warning, Err, Crit, Alert, and Emerg.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=45)** When a priority is listed it shows all messages with this priority and higher.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=50)** In this list Debug is the lowest priority and Emerg being the highest.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=54)** For instance, if we set the priority to Warning it will also show Err, Crit, Alert, and Emerg.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=60)** If I wanted to specify one specific priority I would use the equals sign before it.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=65)** For instance, mail.=crit This would only show critical mail entries, not alerts or emergencies.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=72)** If we want to exclude a certain priority we can use an exclamation point.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=75)** For instance, mail.!crit If we wanted to include all priorities we can use an asterisk to select all.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=84)** Rsyslog also supports property-based filters.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=87)** A property-based filter lets you filter messages based on a property such as text inside the message or the host it came from.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=94)** For instance, :msg, contains, "openvpn"
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=102)** This would filter any messages that contain the word openvpn.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=106)** As well as contains we can also based on: Isequal, Startswith, Regex, Eregex, which is for extended regular expressions, and Isempty.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=118)** For instance, we could filter out hosting by typing in :hostname, isequal, "server1"
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=129)** We can also do expression-based filters.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configuration-of-logrotate?u=76281980&t=132)** For instance, this example filters messages by the openvpn program which are logged to /var/log/openvpn.log Also if the message contains the word init it will get saved in /var/log/init.log If it doesn't contain init then it's saved in /var/log/noinit.log

#### Rsyslog actions
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For every filter, there's an action.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=2)** The action for a log filter usually involves saving the log messages to a file.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=6)** For instance, in the case of cron* /var/log/cron.log, all cron messages are saved to the var.log/cron.log file.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=16)** As this line is written, this file is synchronized on every write.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=19)** This can create a lot of overhead if there are a lot of writes to the log.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=23)** To keep from syncing the file system every time the log is written, we can precede the path with a dash.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=28)** Be aware that the data's held in RAM since it's not read to the disk right away.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=31)** This opens the possibility that you may lose data if the server shuts down unexpectedly.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=37)** Log save paths can either be static as we saw in our previous example or dynamic.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=41)** With a dynamic path, we can create a template and then use that template name, prefixed with a question mark, in the rule.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=47)** The template for this example might look like this.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=50)** It will create a new log file for each message based on the timestamp of the message.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=54)** When creating templates, we can access properties of the message.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=58)** A property could be the text of the message, the host name, message priority, and so on.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=62)** To see the entire list of message priorities, view the main page for rsslog.conf and search for available properties.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=70)** A simple example would be %msg%, which would be the entire message text.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=75)** Inside of the properties, we can also specify a character range.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=78)** To specify the first 10 characters of the message, we'd use %msg:1:10%.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=85)** After the range, we can include a property option.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=88)** This is used to transform the text or format it.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=90)** A great example might be lowercase.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/rsyslog-actions?u=76281980&t=93)** Here's an example template that logs a message a severity, facility, timestamp, host name, message text, and a new line.

#### Clear old systemd journal data
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] By default, the systemd journal is not persistent and only runs in RAM.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=5)** However, as soon as you make it persistent, the journals can stack up, consuming large amounts of disk space.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=10)** In a previous video, we made our journal persistent by creating the /var/log/journal directory and restarting the systemd-journald service.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=20)** We haven't been using our system with a persistent journal for very long, so our journals won't be very large.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=25)** However, let's take a look.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=27)** Type in sudo space journalctl space dash dash disk dash usage and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=34)** This shows we're only using 16 megabytes of space.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=37)** On a production system, this may end up being gigabytes or more.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=41)** You can also use the du command to get the size of the journal directory.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=44)** Type in sudo space du space dash sh for summary and human readable space slash var slash log slash journal and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=54)** Note the du command only shows the amount of disk space used by the archived journal files, not the current journal in RAM, which dash dash disk dash usage shows.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=64)** Depending on if you have archived journals or not, you may see a difference in the output of these two commands.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=70)** There are a couple of ways of clearing out journal files that you don't need.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=73)** One method is to delete them based on how old they are.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=75)** Let's check the age of our files.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=77)** Type in ls space dash l space slash var slash log slash journal slash asterisk and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=85)** This shows my oldest journal file is from January 5.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=88)** This is also my current journal file since I have no archived journals.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=92)** Again, in a production system, you may have journals for weeks or months taking up space.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=97)** If we have archived journals, we can delete them based on age.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=101)** If you wanted to clean up journals from more than a month ago, we type in sudo space journalctl space dash dash vacuum dash time equals 30d.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=110)** This is going to vacuum up journal files older than 30 days.
>

>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=118)** Another way we can manage old journal files is to delete them when they reach a certain size.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=122)** To delete journal files when they reach one gigabyte, type in sudo space journalctl space dash dash vacuum dash size equals one capital G.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=132)** This will delete any file larger than one gigabyte.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=135)** You can also delete all but a certain number of journal files.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=138)** If we wanted to delete all but two journal files, we'd type in sudo space journalctl space dash dash vacuum dash files equals two.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=147)** There are multiple ways of slimming down our journal files.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/clear-old-systemd-journal-data?u=76281980&t=149)** Use the one best suited for your situation.

#### Retrieve systemd journal data from a rescue system
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are times where a server does crash we need to read the data from our recovered drive.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=5)** For normal logging with our syslog or other equivalents, it's easy.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=9)** We just mount the recovered drive into our file system and use grep to find what we want.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=14)** This works because the syslog's messages are text files.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=17)** However, the systemd-journald is binary so we can't do this.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=21)** In order for us to access journals from a recovered system, they need be persistent.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=25)** You make your journal persistent by creating a /var/log/journal directory.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=30)** After restarting the system D dash journal D service, it creates a directory in /var/log/journal named with the UID or universally Unique Identification Number.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=40)** This is different for your system.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=43)** When you use journalctl it automatically reads the journal at /var/log/journal.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=49)** And then, a directory named with your UID.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=52)** Inside that directory, can be one or more journals depending on other factors like the number of users.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=58)** If we restore a journal from a recovered system with a different UID, then journalctl won't read it automatically.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=65)** To get journalctl to read journal files in a different directory, we can use the dash dash directory option.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=71)** Let's say we've inserted the recovered drive into our system, and matter of the root file system has /media/recovered.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=78)** This means the recovered system journal directory path would be at /media/recovered/var/log/journal.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=85)** To use this directory for journalctl operations type, sudo space journalctl space --directory= and then the path to the recovered journal directory.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=96)** To get journalctl to read a different file in any location, we can specify it by path.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=102)** For instance, if we wanted to read the journal at /var/log/journal/recovered.journal we'd type in sudo space journalctl space --file= and then the path to the recovered journal file, and it will read it.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=117)** Another option is to place the recovered system's entire EUID directory in /var/log/journal.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=123)** And the use the -m option to merge all logs in the journal directory.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=128)** To do so, type in sudo space journalctl space -m.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/retrieve-systemd-journal-data-from-a-rescue-system?u=76281980&t=132)** This treats all journals in the journal directory as one, allowing us to read and search through them.

#### About syslog, rsyslog, and syslog-ng
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Syslog, otherwise known as sysklogd, came into being in 1980.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=5)** At that time, it could log single lines to a log file and specify the facility or service that the message is about, plus a log level and a message.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=14)** It could log to remote servers over UDP protocol, but did not have congestion control.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=20)** The syslog protocol became the de facto standard in the Linux and Unix world.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=24)** Syslog had many limitations, so it needed to be replaced.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=28)** 24 years after syslog came out, the rsyslog project was started, and is backwards compatible with sysklogd.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=35)** Rsyslog supports timestamps with millisecond granularity and time zone information.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=41)** It also supports the addition of the name of relays in the host fields to make it possible to track the path a given message has traversed.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=47)** It supports reliable transport using TCP, supports GSS-API and TLS for security, and logging directly into various database engines.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=56)** It also supports RELP, or the reliable event logging protocol, which has a back channel and communicates back to the source to let it know if the message arrived.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=66)** It has support for buffered operation modes where messages are buffered locally if the receiver is not ready.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=72)** Plus, complete input/output support for the systemd journal and content-based filtering, as well as rich filtering capabilities.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=80)** In short, rsyslog is a modernized and more powerful version of sysklogd.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=85)** In 2007, rsyslog became the default in Fedora.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=89)** Fedora was the first distribution to move to rsyslog.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=92)** From 2009 on, openSUSE has used rsyslog as well as Red Hat Enterprise Linux 5 and newer.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=98)** It's also the default in both Debian and Ubuntu.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=102)** It's important to note that rsyslog is backwards compatible with sysklogd making it an easy transition for anyone who already knows how to administer sysklogd.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=112)** Syslog-ng, however, is a new stack written from the ground up starting in 1998.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=117)** It still uses the syslog protocol, but has extended it just like rsyslog has.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=122)** Like rsyslog, it features content-based filtering, rich filtering capabilities, flexible configuration options, and adds important features to syslog, like using TCP for transport.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=133)** Syslog-ng comes in an open-source version, but also a proprietary version as well, and it can log directly into a database.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=140)** In addition, it has the ability to format log messages using Unix shell-like variable expansion.
>
> **[2:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=146)** It can use this variable expansion when naming files covering multiple destination files with a single statement.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=152)** It has the ability to send log messages to local applications, support for message flow control and network transport, and it can rewrite portions of the syslog message with set and substitute primitives.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=163)** It can classify incoming log messages and at the same time extract structured information from unstructured syslog messages.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=171)** It has generic name-value support.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=174)** Each message is just a set of name-value pairs, which can be used to store extra information.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=179)** It has the ability to process structured message formats transmitted over syslog.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=184)** For instance, it can extract columns from CSV formatted lines.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=188)** This means it can handle multi-line messages.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=191)** And lastly, the ability to correlate multiple incoming messages to form a more complex correlated event.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=197)** In short, syslog-ng is a very powerful system logger.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=201)** It can handle logs and multi-line structured formats sent over reliable connections.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-syslog-rsyslog-and-syslog-ng?u=76281980&t=207)** Most distributions, however, do not use it and it doesn't currently support systemd's journal.

### 8. Mail Transfer Agent (MTA) Basics

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### About email
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=0)** - Email was the killer application that drove the creation of the internet as we know it.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=4)** It's still the most popular application on the internet, although now we have many other ways of communicating.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=9)** Electronic mail works in a similar way to snail mail, in that we send letters or messages from one person to another which are delivered by multiple carriers between the two.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=17)** Let's cover some terms associated with email systems.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=21)** With email, the protocol that sends from one email server to another is SMTP, or Simple Mail Transfer Protocol.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=29)** The SMTP Protocol runs on IP for host addressing and uses port number 25.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=34)** Just as a traditional letter has a sender and destination address, an email has a sender's ID and a domain, along with a destination email address.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=43)** It also carries a subject line and the message itself.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=47)** The email system compromises of a mail user agent or MUA, which is the program that sends the mail from the user's computer to a mail transport agent or MTA.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=57)** A mail transport agent or MTA is responsible for delivering the email from the sending mail server to the ending mail server.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=64)** A mail submission agent or MSA is responsible for accepting new mail for the MTA.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=70)** This functionality may be included in the MTA or MUA software applications or it may be a separate program.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=78)** A mail delivery agent or MDA is responsible for delivering incoming messages to the mail spool.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=84)** This also may be integrated in the MTA or it may be a separate program.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=89)** We've already discussed the SMTP protocol used by mail user agents to send mail to MTAs, it's also used between MTAs.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=97)** However, for downloading mail from the MTA, there are two common protocols used by the mail user agent.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=103)** The first is Post Office Protocol or POP.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=106)** The last version of POP was POP3 and it uses port number 110.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=110)** By default, POP deletes the email from the server when you download it.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=114)** Although there are some POP clients that will optionally leave the mail there.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=118)** It's also possible to use a secure version of POP called POP3s, which uses SSL and port number 995.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=126)** The other protocol for downloading email is Internet Mail Access Protocol or IMAP.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=131)** IMAP is more powerful than POP in that it's easier to access mail using multiple systems, and works better over slow connections.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=138)** Because IMAP only downloads a copy of the email, a user can log into a different host and retrieve their email again.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=145)** A smart host or mail relay is an MTA configured to deliver mail for another system.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=150)** The other system may not have a caustic connection to the internet, so it delivers mail to the relay, which then sends it on.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=157)** A mail queue is a directory where mail is stored while it's waiting to be processed.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=162)** And lastly, a mailbox is a place where user mail is stored.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-email?u=76281980&t=166)** A mailbox is created for each user.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>

> **Env Vars:** mta (7), pop (6), smtp (3), imap (3), mua (2)
> **Definitions:** is a  (2), is an  (1)

#### How email works
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/how-email-works?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/how-email-works?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] It helps to visualize how email systems work.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/how-email-works?u=76281980&t=3)** Let's go through the process of an email going from one user to another.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/how-email-works?u=76281980&t=7)** The mail is composed and then sent on by the mail user agent using SMTP.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/how-email-works?u=76281980&t=12)** The mail submission agent that resides on the mail server accepts the message and adds it to the mail queue for further processing.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/how-email-works?u=76281980&t=18)** The mail transport agent on the mail server then transports that email to another mail server on the network or internet using the SMTP protocol.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/how-email-works?u=76281980&t=27)** The MTA receives the mail, and the mail delivery agent stores it in a mail spool, waiting for the mail user agent to retrieve it.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/how-email-works?u=76281980&t=34)** Lastly, the MTA authenticates the mail user agent, which downloads the mail message using either POP3 or IMAP protocols.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** smtp (2), mta (2), pop3 (1), imap (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Knowledge of common MTA programs
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=0)** A Mail Transport Agent or MTA is a program which transports email between mail servers.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=6)** There are a lot of MTA's available for Linux.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=8)** In this video, we'll discuss a few.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=10)** The old standby and first killer application for the internet is Sendmail.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=14)** Sendmail came out in 1983, and by 1996, had 80% of the MTA market share.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=20)** As recently as 2005, Sendmail had fallen to 35%, and as of this recording, was down to 4%.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=28)** Sendmail is big and difficult to configure, and complex to secure.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=32)** There aren't many Sendmail servers of production these days.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=35)** Postfix came out in 1998, about the time that Sendmail had near total dominance of the MTA market.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=40)** It was created by IBM as a drop-in replacement for Sendmail.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=44)** And was created in response to Sendmail's security and configuration issues.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=49)** It has easy to understand configuration files, it supports multiple domains, encryption, and so on.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=55)** Postfix is not monolithic, like Sendmail and has multiple independent binaries to spread the load.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=60)** Postfix currently has about 30% of the MTA market share, and is the default for most Linux distributions.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=67)** Qmail was released in 1995 and is a secure, reliable, and efficient MTA.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=73)** It was was also developed as a replacement for Sendmail due to security issues and configuration complexity.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=79)** Qmail's claim to fame is that it has no known security issues.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=83)** It has not been updated in many years, for it lives on packaged in indie mail and open sourced mail server platforms.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=90)** Qmail is public domain and has negligible market share.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=93)** The last alternative MTA that we'll discuss is Exim.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=97)** Exim was created at the University of Cambridge and first released in 1995.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=102)** Exim is the most popular MTA in the world and commands 57% of the market.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=108)** Exim follows the Sendmail design philosophy, with one large binary handling all functions of the MTA.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=114)** It has also had a long history of security vulnerabilities, however, it has many configuration options and abilities that other MTA's don't have, and is easy to configure.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=124)** Thus, contributing to it's popularity.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=126)** Because of how popular Sendmail was, almost every MTA has a Sendmail emulation layer.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=131)** So it can function in place of Sendamil to maintain backwards compatibility.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=135)** Often, you can type Sendmail or mail-q on the command line of a system that doesn't have Sendmail installed and they will run.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=142)** Usually, there are different commands serving the same functions as the originals.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=148)** Since we'll be moving into a networked environment and we'll be using both VM's now, let's change their host names to make it easier to tell them apart.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=155)** Log in to your rhs1 vm, type in sudo hostname ctl set-hostname rhhost1.[localnet.com](https://localnet.com)
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=168)** and hit enter.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=169)** Type in your password as prompted.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=171)** You'll have to close your terminal and open it again to see the results.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=175)** Also, log in to you rhhost2 vm, and set it's hostname to rhhost2.[localnet.com](https://localnet.com).
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-common-mta-programs?u=76281980&t=182)** Now, when we're on the terminal, we can identify the host we're logged into easier.

#### Configure a local mail server
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-a-local-mail-server?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-a-local-mail-server?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To configure a local mail server, we'll want to start by editing the etsy Postfix main dot c-f file using VI.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-a-local-mail-server?u=76281980&t=6)** On your rhhost1 VM open a terminal and type in sudo, space, v-I, space, slash, e-t-c, slash, Postfix, slash, main dot c-f and hit enter.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-a-local-mail-server?u=76281980&t=15)** Enter your password if prompted.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-a-local-mail-server?u=76281980&t=18)** The first parameter we'll edit is my host name.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-a-local-mail-server?u=76281980&t=20)** Press forward slash and type in myhostname and hit enter.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-a-local-mail-server?u=76281980&t=24)** Go into insert mode and change the value to rhhost1.[localnet.com](https://localnet.com).
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-a-local-mail-server?u=76281980&t=29)** And make sure it's uncommented.

#### Create email aliases
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The "Aliases" file is a file that forwards email from one recipient to another local recipient.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=5)** Unlike the other files we've looked at, this file has non-commented working lines.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=9)** So let's take a look at it.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=11)** On your Rhhost1 VM in a terminal, type in, "less /etc/aliases" and hit "Enter".
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=18)** Notice the path to this file is different than the others, as it's an Etsy and not an Etsy post fix.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=24)** The format is pretty simple.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=26)** Any mail sent to the recipient on the left, gets forwarded to the recipient on the right.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=30)** Notice that there are a lot of service names.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=32)** Any mail sent to the names of these services, will get forwarded to the root account.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=36)** So the root user will see them.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=38)** Notice at the very bottom of the file, we can also decide gets the emails to root.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=43)** Since it's not a good idea for someone to be logged in as root, we can have all emails to root be forwarded to a user account.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=49)** In our case, maybe we'd want them sent to the user named "user1".
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=53)** Another interesting use is that we could forward emails for accounts that don't exist.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=57)** Let's say a user no longer works for a company, but there still may be email coming in for that person.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=62)** We can forward that email to a current user, that took over his or her job.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=65)** For instance, "Bob user1".
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=67)** In this case Bob no longer works for the company, so we have his email forwarded to user1, who took over his job.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=73)** Another possibility is to create an email account for a group of users.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=77)** Say, support.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=78)** Then forward that email to each person doing that job.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=81)** In this case, "support user1 user2 user3".
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=85)** Any email sent to support will get forwarded to user1, user2 and user3.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=90)** Before this file can be used, we need to convert it to an indexed database file.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=94)** But we don't use the postmap command for it.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=96)** We have a special command just for the Aliases file, called "newaliases".

>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=104)** And the aliases.db file will be created.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=108)** We can verify by typing in, " ls /etc/aliases* " and hit "Enter".
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=115)** Because this file is in the "/etc", instead of "/etc/postfix" we want to be more specific in listing just the alias's files.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/create-email-aliases?u=76281980&t=123)** So we use the asterisk.

#### Forward mail as a user
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] System wide email aliases are stored in the /etc/aliases file and must be managed by the system administrator.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=7)** This file must be compiled by the newaliases command to create an aliases database before it can be used.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=13)** We can do this just by running the newaliases command without options.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=16)** Host space looks into its configuration files to figure out where the aliases file is.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=20)** However, regular users can forward email of their own by using a .forward file in their home directory.
>
> **[0:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=27)** This is their individual email forwarding file.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=30)** As such, the user doesn't have to be root to configure it.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=34)** By using a .forward file, the user can forward to a local user or even an outside email address.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=38)** Take note that this file needs to be readable by the user.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=42)** The easiest way to ensure this is to create it as that user.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=45)** This is the simple as creating a file named .forward with a text editor, including anything that we would've put in the right hand side of the aliases file.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=53)** For instance, if user one wanted all of their email forward to their personal account on Gmail, they'd just type that address into their .forward file.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=61)** When mail is destined for the user, the MTA looks at the .forward file to see if any additional processing is necessary.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=68)** If there's a forward in that file, the MTA will send the mail on in the same manner as if it had been in the aliases file.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/forward-mail-as-a-user?u=76281980&t=75)** Unlike the aliases file, this does not need to be compiled and will be consulted each time email comes in for that user.

#### Configure local and remote printers with CUPS
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Early computers used line printers which printed one line at a time.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=3)** The printers were slow, so there needed to be a print queue or spool to line up print jobs.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=8)** The print queue or spool would line up print jobs from multiple computers and give them to the printer as the printer was available.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=14)** Initially Linux used BSD's printing system, which comprised of a service called LPD, or Line Printer Daemon.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=21)** There were clients such as LPR that would send print jobs to the print server using the LPD protocol.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=26)** When graphics printing came out, there needed to be a way to tell the printer how to print graphical images.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=31)** So the PostScript language was created.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=33)** Users would create a graphical document in either black and white or color which would be saved as a PostScript file.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=38)** PostScript is a programming language that describes the page.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=41)** The computer would send this PostScript file to the printer.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=43)** High end printers had PostScript engines in them called RIPS or raster image processors.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=49)** That would take the code and run it just the same as we would with bash or Python code on a computer.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=54)** The code would run and output the file in the printer's native language and the printer would print the page.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=59)** Cheaper printers were sold without raster image processors.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=63)** And as such, they needed computers that had software raster image processors built in.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=67)** The software would save the graphic output as a PostScript file.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=71)** The raster image processor would process the PostScript file into the printers own language, and then it would send that process data to the printer to be printed.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=79)** This piece of software that converts the PostScript file to the printers language is called a print filter.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=84)** The print system used on modern Linux distributions is CUPS, originally short for common Unix printing system, but changed a CUPS over legal issues concerning the name UNIX, and it's late caps use the LPD protocol but switched to IPP or Internet Printing Protocol.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=99)** IPP supports printing to local and remote printers access control as indication and encryption.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-and-remote-printers-with-cups?u=76281980&t=106)** 10 years later, Apple boughtCUPS from the developer and hired him.

#### Troubleshoot general printing problems
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/troubleshoot-general-printing-problems?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/troubleshoot-general-printing-problems?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In a network environment where multiple users on multiple hosts may be directing print jobs to one central print queue, it might get complex.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/troubleshoot-general-printing-problems?u=76281980&t=7)** To help troubleshoot, we can inspect and manipulate print queues using some very basic commands.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/troubleshoot-general-printing-problems?u=76281980&t=12)** To get a list of print queues known to the system, type into a terminal, lpstat space dash a, and hit Enter.
>
