### 10. Fundamentals of Internet Protocols

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### About private and public "dotted quad" IP addresses
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Each computer or device on a TCP/IP network will have an IP address.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=4)** This could be an IP version four address or an IP version six address.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=8)** We won't go into details about the differences in this video, but just know that every device has an address of either type, or both.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=14)** IP version four has a 32 bit address space, meaning there are roughly four billion IP version four addresses.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=20)** However, there are 18 million addresses reserved for private space, and 270 million addresses reserved for multicast networks.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=29)** Each IP version four address comprises of four dotted quad octets.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=34)** They're called octets because they're made up of eight bits each.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=37)** Four octets of eight bits makes 32 bits total.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=40)** These octets are delimited by dots.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=42)** Since an octet is an eight bit number, its values range from zero to 255 in decimal notation.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=48)** There are several ranges of private IP addresses.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=51)** These are 192.168.0.0 to 192.168.255.255, which comprises 65,000 IP addresses.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=60)** 172.16.0.0 to 172.31.255.255 which comprises of about one million IP addresses 10.0.0.0 to 10.255.255.255, which comprises about 16 million addresses.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=78)** These are private address ranges and are not routable across the internet.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=81)** This means they're free for anyone to use as long as these addresses are translated to public addresses before the data enters the public Internet.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=88)** We usually do this with NAT or network address translation.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=92)** There are some other address blocks that we can't use for public networking.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=95)** The address range of 169.254.0.0 to 169.254.255.255 is only used for link local addressing, meaning they're used when an IP address can't be assigned automatically.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=109)** Every host also has non-routable addresses called loopback addresses.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=113)** Those addresses are from 127.0.0.0 - 127.255.255.255 These addresses are only used inside of our computer and are never routed over a network.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=123)** Any packets received on the loopback address must be dropped because our host should never receive one outside its own network interface.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=131)** Addresses ending in zero or 255 are also special.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=134)** The dot zero addresses define the network or sub network and addresses ending in .255 are for the broadcast address.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=141)** The broadcast address is one address that lets data be sent to every host on the network.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=145)** Generally, we are given a public IP address from our internet service provider, which is assigned to our router.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=151)** Behind the router we use one of these private address ranges for our local hosts.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=155)** When a local host wants to communicate with the outside public world, their address is translated to the public address.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=161)** Return traffic is then translated back to the private address.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-private-and-public-dotted-quad-ip-addresses?u=76281980&t=165)** This lets us get by with fewer public addresses, as most computers and internet cafes, private homes, and business offices are using private addresses behind NATs.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** data (2), traffic (1), business (1)
> **Versions:** 192.168.0 (1), 192.168.255 (1), 172.16.0 (1), 172.31.255 (1), 10.0.0 (1)
> **Env Vars:** tcp (1), nat (1)
> **Definitions:** is an  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### Network masks and CIDR notation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=0)** - With IPv4 addresses, there are four octets of eight bits each, making up a 32-bit number.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=6)** In decimal, this ranges from 0.0.0.0 to 255.255.255.255 It's a bit clumsy to work with in decimal, so let's convert it to binary.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=18)** The numeric range in binary is 00000000.00000000.00000000.00000000 to 11111111.11111111.11111111.11111111 This might not look better, but it'll make more sense when we start breaking up networks into sub networks.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=32)** Initially, the network portion of an IP address started with a zero as its higher-order bit and used seven bits of the first octet for the network address.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=40)** This means we had seven bits left for networks, which gave us 127 networks.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=45)** The rest of the IP address space was used for hosts.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=48)** Since we have 24 bits for hosts, we have around 16 million hosts.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=52)** This was fine when we only had a few companies needing very large networks.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=55)** But it became clear that we need more networks and they didn't have to be this big.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=59)** In 1981 the address range was divided into classes.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=63)** The original networks were now Class A networks.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=66)** The next class would be Class B.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=68)** Class B use 16 bits for the network portion and 16 bits for the host portion.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=73)** It used the binary number 10 as its high-order bits, which left 14 bits for network addresses.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=78)** This means we have 16,384 Class B networks and 65,536 hosts on each.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=85)** We don't need to reserve bits on the host portion, so we get the full 16 bits worth.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=90)** The third class is Class C.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=92)** Class C uses 24 bits for the network portion and eight bits for hosts.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=96)** It uses the binary number 110 as its higher-order bits.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=101)** This leaves 21 bits left for networks or roughly two million.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=105)** It uses eight bits for host or 256 in decimal.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=108)** Keep in mind that for each network the lowest number is the network address and the highest is the broadcast.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=113)** So we need to subtract two from each of these host numbers.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=117)** The Class D networks are reserved for multicasting.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=120)** The Class E networks are defined as experimental.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=123)** We have another number called the network mask, which is used to determine how much of the address spaces is for networks and how much is for hosts.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=130)** For Class A, the network portion is eight bits.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=133)** If we put ones in every spot that the network occupies our network mask in binary would be 11111111.00000000.00000000.00000000 or 255.0.0.0 in decimal.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=145)** For Class B networks it would be 11111111.11111111.00000000.00000000 or 255.255.0.0 For Class C it would be three octets of ones and then one octet of zeros or in decimal 255.255.255.0 There may be times where you don't want all of your hosts in the same network, but you are only given one network.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=167)** In this case, you can subnet or divide your network.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=170)** This means that you take one bit from the host section and add it to the network section to double the number of networks to two and half the number of hosts on each network.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=178)** In this case, our network mask in binary would look like this.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=182)** If we convert the binary number one, followed by seven zeros, into decimal, we end up with 128.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=188)** So our network mask in decimal would be 255.255.255.128.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=194)** We'd be using the one extra bit for our network addresses, which gives us two networks and seven bits for host, which gives us 128 hosts each.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=202)** Once again, keep in mind that the lowest host number is the network address and the highest is the broadcast.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=207)** Since we have two networks, we're now using four numbers for networks and broadcast addresses.
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=211)** Let's say we apply this to the 192.168.2.0 network.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=216)** Normally, 192.168.2.0 would be the network address and 192.168.2.255 would be the broadcast.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=224)** Since we have now divided this into two networks, the first network's addresses would go from 192.168.2.0 to 192.168.2.127 Whereas zero being the network address and 127 the broadcast.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=239)** Usable IPs would go from one through 126.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=243)** The second network would comprise of 192.168.2.128 to 192.168.2.255 With 128 being the network address and 255 the broadcast.
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=256)** Usable IPs would number from 129 to 254.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=260)** The subnet mask can be written in two different forms.
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=262)** As dotted-quad notation or CIDR, which is short for classless inter-domain routing.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=268)** To convert to dotted notation, let's look at our last octet.
>
> **[4:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=272)** If we subnet, we're stealing one bit from the host and putting it in the network section.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=276)** So, we need to convert to decimal, 11111111.11111111.11111111.10000000 This binary number converted to decimal is 255.255.255.128 because the one followed by seven zeros converted to decimal is 128.
>
> **[4:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=293)** This is our network mask in dotted-quad notation.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=297)** With CIDR, we just append the number of bits in the network mask to the IP address.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=301)** So, ours would be /25.
>
> **[5:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=303)** For instance, our IP and subnet mask could be 192.168.2.100/25 Simple, right?
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=311)** If you do a slash 26 subnet, you now have four networks with 64 hosts and each and so on.
>
> **[5:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=317)** CIDR is very easy to work with.
>
> **[5:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/network-masks-and-cidr-notation?u=76281980&t=319)** I recommend that you think in CIDR and then convert back to dotted notation whenever you need.

#### Common TCP and UDP ports and services

>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=4)** There needs to be an addressing scheme, inside of our computers to determine, what happens to that traffic, once it arrives at our host and there is.

> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=32)** Once it arrives at the local network stack, it will be directed to the service listening on port 80.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=37)** In our example, the web server.

> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=39)** The network socket for this combination would be, 192.168.2.100 colon 80, as port 80 is the default port for web servers.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=48)** Every packet has a source address, and a source port, a destination address and a destination port.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=54)** Many destination ports are defined by IANA, a government standards organization.

> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=59)** IANA stands for the Internet Assigned Numbers Authority.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=63)** The ports are represented by a 16 bit number, meaning there are roughly 65, 000 of them.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=68)** The lower 1024 ports are called, the Well-Known Ports, and are reserved for, common communication protocols.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=74)** For instance, ports 20 and 21 are used for FTP, port 22 is used for Secure Shell, port 23 is used for telnet, port 25 is used for SMTP mail, port 53 is used for DNS, and port 80 is used for web servers.

> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/common-tcp-and-udp-ports-and-services?u=76281980&t=103)** Here we can scroll through this file.

#### Differences and major features of UDP, TCP, and ICMP
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] With TCP/IP, there are different transport protocols.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=3)** These are TCP and UDP.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=5)** Both TCP and UDP are used to transport payloads of data.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=8)** Both use IP addressing, and are forwarded from host to host until they get to their destination.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=13)** You can think of TCP and UDP as the trucks that carry packages to destination addresses, although they are different in how they do it.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=21)** TCP, or Transmission Control Protocol, is a connection-oriented protocol, meaning it is considered reliable.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=28)** It has a three-way handshake to insure that there is reliable communication between hosts.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=32)** TCP provides acknowledgments after successful delivery of packets, and automatic re-sends in the case of missing data.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=38)** TCP also cares about the ordering of the packets, so it reassembles the packets at the destination, in order to preserve the integrity of the data.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=45)** It uses error detection and error recovery.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=49)** There are checksums on each packet.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=51)** If the packet arrives damaged, it's resent.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=54)** UDP or User Datagram Protocol on the other hand is not connection-oriented, nor does it care about the sequence of data.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=61)** With UDP, there's no tracking of messages and no way of knowing if the packet was delivered or not.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=66)** UDP does pay attention to checksums on the data, but if it finds a damaged packet, it just discards it, so it uses error detection, but not error recovery.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=75)** This might seem like a crazy idea, but think for a moment about streaming movies.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=79)** If a frame gets corrupted, do you want the movie to stop while that corrupted frame is re-sent?
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=83)** Probably not.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=84)** It's better to drop it and keep the movie going.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=86)** There's a third low-level protocol called ICMP, or Internet Control Message Protocol.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=92)** ICMP is an error-reporting protocol that network devices use to generate error messages.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=97)** For instance, if a packet has not been delivered due to a network problem, we'll see an ICMP message.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=102)** All network devices have the capability to send, receive, and process ICMP messages.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=108)** It's important to note that ICMP is not a transport protocol like TCP or UDP, and doesn't carry data between hosts.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=115)** ICMP is not just a protocol for network devices, it can also be used by network admins to troubleshoot network connections using diagnostic tools, such as ping and traceroute.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=125)** In summary, TCP is used when reliability is more important than speed.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=130)** UDP is used for speed, and when reliability isn't an issue.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/differences-and-major-features-of-udp-tcp-and-icmp?u=76281980&t=133)** And ICMP is used to relay error messages and troubleshoot networks.

#### Knowledge of the major differences between IPv4 and IPv6
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In the late 1990s, we thought we would be transitioning to IP version six as it had become a draft standard.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=6)** It remained a draft standard for nearly 20 years and only as of 2017 became a full standard.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=12)** The world is still mostly running on IP version four.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=16)** One of the issues with IP version four is that it has a limited number of possible IP addresses.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=21)** Although 4.3 billion seems like a lot, we probably exhausted that a long time ago.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=26)** Most internal networks use network address translation to make one public IP address appear as many, possibly thousands.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=33)** This has gotten us by so far.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=35)** However, IP version six has many advantages, including 340 trillion trillion trillion addresses.
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=42)** That's not really a number but the real number of 340 undecillion may not mean a lot to anyone.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=48)** IP version six also has other advantages over IP version four, including stateless address autoconfiguration, network renumbering, and router announcements when changing connectivity providers.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=59)** It places packet fragmentation into the endpoints and also network security was a design requirement.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=66)** You may be asking why we haven't moved to IP version six already if it's so much better.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=70)** Part of the issue may be that people keep doing what they know.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=74)** Another thing may be that the addresses look like this, which aren't very readable.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=78)** Rules exist to improve the situation though.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=80)** All leading zeroes in a group can be deleted.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=83)** This shortens the address quite a lot.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=85)** We can also remove successive groups of zeroes, thus collapsing the address again.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=89)** This results in an even shorter address.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=92)** This is much easier to use and possibly remember but still not that user-friendly.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=97)** Linux has had IP version six turned on by default for at least a decade or more.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/knowledge-of-the-major-differences-between-ipv4-and-ipv6?u=76281980&t=101)** It's good to take advantage of that capability if possible.

#### Manual network interface configuration
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=0)** Network interface configuration files control the interfaces for individual network devices.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=5)** As the system boots, these files are read and the network interfaces are configured or possibly ignored, depending on the configuration.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=12)** These files are stored in /etc/sysconfig/network-scripts and named ifcg-name where name is the name of the network interface.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=23)** For instance, the configuration file for the enp0s3 network interface would be /etc/sysconfig/network-scripts/ifcfg-enp0s3.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=37)** The network interface is also named inside of the file as well.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=40)** Generally it's a good idea to have them match.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=43)** These scripts are for all network interface configuration including dynamic and manual configs.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=48)** A dynamic config may have only few lines in it specifying the name of the device and the boot protocol of DHCP.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=55)** This example specifies that it should be enabled at boot and uses DHCP for configuration.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=61)** To create a static interface configuration, we'll change the boot protocol to none.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=66)** In this case, we're using a 24-bit subnet mask in the prefix line.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=70)** If we wanted to insert the netmask as dotted notation, we could write it as netmask equals 255.255.255.0.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=79)** The last line is the statically assigned IP version four address.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=83)** There are a lot of different options you can add to this file.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=85)** Let's go over some of the more common ones.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=88)** Device specifies the network device to configure.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=91)** IPADDR is for the IP version four address.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=95)** IPV6ADDR is for the IP version six address.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=99)** The HWADDR option specifies which network interface to configure if you have more than one.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=106)** This is used to identify the network interface.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=109)** The MACADDR option assigns a new MAC-address overwriting the one on the card.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=114)** BOOTPROTO assigns the boot protocol and can be none for static assignments, bootp for older dynamic assignments, and dhcp for newer dynamic networks.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=124)** ONBOOT specifies whether the network interface should be enabled at boot.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=128)** IPV6INIT specifies whether IP version six should be enabled or not.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=133)** The next couple need some explanation.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=135)** The global DNS name server file is /etc/resolv.conf.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=140)** However, Enterprise Linux allows interface specific DNS servers using the DNS one equals or DNS two equals options.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=149)** We would specify the DNS server addresses here.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=152)** To keep these options interface specific, we need to set PEERDNS to yes.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=157)** If we change it to no, these DNS servers are then copied to the global/etc/resolv.conf file.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=164)** Netmask and prefix are both used to set the network mask.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=167)** Only one is required.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=169)** If you want to enter the netmask in dotted notation, use NETMASK equals.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=173)** If you want to use CIDR, then use PREFIX equals.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=176)** The next two are items are for whether you want network manager controlling the interface, or whether you want non-root users to be able to control it.
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=183)** There are many other options used to tune network interfaces and provide more control.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=188)** The Red Hat Enterprise Linux six deployment guide actually has more information on these files than the Red Hat Enterprise Linux seven networking guide, so reference that.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=196)** You might want to note that if you change or edit these configuration files, you'll need to notify network manager.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=202)** One way to do this is to use nmcli to reload the configuration.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manual-network-interface-configuration?u=76281980&t=206)** In a terminal, you would type nmcli space c for configuration, space reload and hit enter.

> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-networking-using-the-gui?u=76281980&t=55)** Mine is on dynamic using DHCP.

> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-networking-using-the-gui?u=76281980&t=68)** Here we can add or edit the IP Version 4 address, Netmask and default Gateway.

> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-networking-using-the-gui?u=76281980&t=74)** We can also add more IP addresses like we did on the command line earlier in this course by clicking on the plus button.

> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-networking-using-the-gui?u=76281980&t=79)** We can also add DNS servers and static routes if we wish.

> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-networking-using-the-gui?u=76281980&t=85)** The IP Version 6 section mimics it's IP Version 4 counterpart.

> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-networking-using-the-gui?u=76281980&t=89)** The last section, named Reset, is to clear all settings and forget an interface.

#### Awareness of systemd-networkd
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Networking under Linux is very powerful, but managed by many different tools.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=5)** On Red Hat based distributions, we may edit ifcfg files in /ect/sysconfic/network-scripts.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=12)** On Debian based distributions, we can edit the /ect/network/interfaces file.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=18)** On SUSE, we'd edit ifcfg files in /ect/sysconfig/network.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=24)** For dynamic interfaces, we could use WICD or NetworkManager.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=28)** In recent years, most distributions have moved to NetworkManager, which is still the most powerful dynamic network management stack on Linux.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=35)** However, that may change in the future as the developers of systemd have created networkd.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=41)** Systemd-networkd is a system service that manages networks.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=45)** It detects and configures network devices as they appear, as well as creating virtual network devices.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=50)** Networkd isn't as powerful as NetworkManager, but currently can manage both static and dynamic connections.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=57)** Systemd-networks will look for network configuration files in /usr/lib/systemd/network, /run/systemd/netowrk, and lastly /ect/systemd/network in that order.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=72)** Each configuration found in these directories will have a .network file extension.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=76)** In each file, there will be a match criteria.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=78)** Any devices not matched by a .network file are ignored.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=82)** In this file, there's a section named match, which matches a piece of data such as a network device, name, MAC address, driver, device path, type, SSID, or a property, such as a vendor name, or the model of the card.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=96)** The link section is used to configure low level link information such as ARP or multicast.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=102)** The network section is for configuring the network itself.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=105)** This is equivalent to the ifcfg configuration files.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=108)** The route section is for routing information.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=111)** The DHCP server section is for DHCP server information.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=114)** The bridge section is for configuring network bridges.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=117)** And there are more sections that we won't go over here.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=120)** A simple static network configuration file looks like this.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=123)** I think you can see from this file that the syntax is clean and easy to read.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=128)** The match section at the top matches the network device by name.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=131)** The network section assigns the IP address, netmask, and default gateway.
>
> **[2:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=136)** Note that networkd may need to be installed before the configuration directories will appear.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=140)** In CentOS, if we want to install it, we would type sudo space yum space install space -y space systemd-networkd.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=148)** Also note that systemd-networkd requires systemd-resolved to manage name resolution.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=155)** If you want to switch from NetworkManager to networkd, we stop one service and start the other.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/awareness-of-systemd-networkd?u=76281980&t=160)** With this comes the networkctl command to manage our network interfaces.

### 12. Basic Network Troubleshooting

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### About routing
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Routing is the process that allows one system to find the network path to another.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=5)** Routing is often handled by devices on the network dedicated to routing traffic called routers.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=10)** Although, any computer or programmable network device may be able to act as a router.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=15)** Usually a host doesn't have to have complex routing defined as there are rules for where traffic goes depending on where it comes from.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=22)** Scenario one, if the source and destination hosts are on the same physical network, the traffic is forwarded to the destination directly, usually by the network switch.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=31)** Scenario two, if the source and destination hosts are not on the same physical network, then all defined routes in the routing table are tried one after another.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=40)** These routes could be dynamic routes added by our DHCP server or they could be static routes that we've added manually.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=47)** If a proper route is found, the packet is forwarded.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=50)** Scenario three, if a proper route is not found, then the packet is sent to the default gateway, which would be configured either dynamically via DHCP or manually via the system administrator.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=61)** The default gateway then attempts to find a proper route for the packet and sends it on.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-routing?u=76281980&t=66)** If it cannot, it will send it to its default gateway.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Routing]] (5), traffic (3)
> **CLI Commands:** find (2)
> **Env Vars:** dhcp (2)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### View the routing table
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] To view your routing table, you can be logged into either of your VMs.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=4)** I'm logged into my rhhost2 VM.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=6)** In a terminal type in ip space route and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=10)** Let's look at the different parts of this output.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=13)** The left hand column is the destination network address with subnet mask.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=17)** The word default signifies the default gateway address where traffic is sent if there's no available route.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=23)** The second section encompasses two words, dev and the name of the device for the network.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=28)** This could be a physical or virtual network device.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=32)** The third section also encompasses two words, proto followed by the protocol.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=36)** The kernel protocol says that the kernel installed the route during auto configuration.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=41)** These protocols are defined in the /etc/iproute2/rt_protos file.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=47)** If this section says proto static, it means the route was set by the administrator and overrides the dynamic routing.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=53)** The next section determines the scope of the destination.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=56)** The /etc/iproute2/rt_scopes file defines these.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=63)** The next section is for the source address.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=65)** For many lines, this will be the last piece of data.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=67)** However, you may have another section displaying the metric, this would display the cost of using the route or the number of hops.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/view-the-routing-table?u=76281980&t=75)** Local networks would be one hop and remote networks more.

#### Manually configure routing using iproute2
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=0)** - We can add static routes using the IP route command.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=3)** We can also add static routes using the legacy route command, although it needs to be installed first.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=8)** However, if you want your static routes to survive a reboot, you'll need to add them to configuration files in /etc/sysconfig/network-scripts.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=17)** When we create a network interface configuration file, we name it ifcfg-interface name, such as ifcfg-eth0.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=26)** For static routes, we name the configuration file route-interface name.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=30)** For instance, if we wanted to add a static route for the eth0 interface, we'd create a file called route-eth0.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=38)** To add a route manually using the IP command, we'd type ip route add 192.168.100.0, which is the network address, /24 which is the subnet mask, 192.168.100.1, which is the gateway, dev team0, which is the interface.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=60)** If you typed in ip route, you would see the results of this line.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=64)** The legacy route version of this command line is a bit longer.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=67)** We'd type in route add -net 192.168.100.0 netmask space 255.255.255.0 gw for gateway, space 192.168.100.1 dev team0.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=87)** However, if you reboot after setting the static route with either of these commands, it will be gone.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=91)** To make it persistent, we'll want to add it to a configuration file.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=95)** As such, we'd create a route-team0 file in /etc/sysconfig/network-scripts.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=102)** Inside this file, we'd add address0 equals 192.168.100.0.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=108)** Netmask equals 255.255.255.0.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=113)** Gateway equals 192.168.100.1.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=118)** The device name is derived from the file name itself.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/manually-configure-routing-using-iproute2?u=76281980&t=120)** This would be equivalent to a manual IP route example earlier, but it would survive a reboot.

> **[5:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/debug-problems-associated-with-the-network-configuration?u=76281980&t=320)** Type in ping space [LinkedIn.com](https://LinkedIn.com) and hit enter.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/debug-problems-associated-with-the-network-configuration?u=76281980&t=325)** If you get a response, then you have proper DNS resolution.
>
> **[5:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/debug-problems-associated-with-the-network-configuration?u=76281980&t=328)** Press control C.

> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=154)** This can be combined with any of the record filters we just discussed.

>

> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=275)** Because we're getting back answers from multiple sites we'll probably want to add the +noall and +answer options as well.

>
> **[5:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/query-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=306)** In this file, just add the options such as +noall space +answer on one line and they will be automatically appended when using dig unless you override them.

#### Configure local name resolution with remote DNS servers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=0)** - There are two client-side name resolution files that we should be concerned with in Enterprise Linux 7.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=6)** Resolve.conf and nsswitch.conf.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=9)** The purpose of resolv.conf is to provide a list of global DNS servers for our client to query when resolving DNS names.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=15)** The format of the file is fairly simple.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=17)** There are two columns.
>
> **[0:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=19)** The first column will have either search, domain, nameserver, sortlist or options in it.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=25)** If it has search, it will be followed by up to six domain names with a total of 256 characters.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=31)** That will be appended if you try and resolve something incomplete.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=34)** For instance, if you try to resolving the word test, and the search line included [localnet.com](https://localnet.com), [ford.com](https://ford.com) and [apple.com](https://apple.com), it would try to resolve test.[localnet.com](https://localnet.com), test.[ford.com](https://ford.com), test.[apple.com](https://apple.com) and lastly, test by itself.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=51)** One of these domains has to be a local domain name.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=55)** If you do not include a search line, then it defaults to your local domain name.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=59)** For instance, if my local domain name was [localnet.com](https://localnet.com) and I tried resolving test, my system would resolve test.[localnet.com](https://localnet.com) first and then try to resolve test second.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=69)** Even if I did not have a search line.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=71)** If the first column specifies domain, it will pin that name to the end of a short name.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=76)** Normally this is our local domain name but if we wanted to change it we could.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=79)** For instance, if we had domain space [otherdomain.com](https://otherdomain.com) and we resolved test, it would try to resolve it to test.[otherdomain.com](https://otherdomain.com).
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=88)** You only need to do this if your local domain is not the same as the rest of your network.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=92)** The keyword we use the most is nameserver.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=95)** The nameserver keyword is followed by a DNS server IP address to query.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=99)** For instance, nameserver space 4.2.2.2.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=103)** You can have up to three nameserver lines and the client will query them in the order they are listed.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=107)** The sortlist keyword allows us to sort IP addresses in certain networks.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=111)** For instance, if we had sortlist space one three zero dot one five five dot one six zero slash two five five dot two five five dot two four zero dot zero it would allow addresses returned to be sorted in this network.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=125)** There can be up to ten network and subnet pairs.

>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=132)** The options keyword let's us set DNS client options, such as, querying the names of his round robin to spread the load, a timeout or the number of attempts the client will query the DNS server before giving up.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=144)** Keep in mind to that if you want to set DNS name servers per network interface, you can do so by adding them into the interface specific configuration files.

> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=153)** The slash ETC slash resolve.conf is a global configuration file.

> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/configure-local-name-resolution-with-remote-dns-servers?u=76281980&t=157)** Also keep in mind that if you set your DNS server's in the interface specific configuration file, and you also set the PEERDNS directive to yes then those DNS servers will get copied to slash ETC slash resolve.conf automatically.

#### About systemd-resolved
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Systemd - Networkd is the new stack to manage network configuration files on Linux.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=5)** Along with this comes systemd that's resolvd.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=8)** Resolvd provides interface specific name resolution.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=11)** Currently, we manage global name resolution configuration in the /etc/resolv.conf file and per interface configuration in each ifcfg network configuration file.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=22)** The current system is fairly rigid and not very dynamic.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=26)** It's not uncommon these days to be connected to more than one dynamic network at a time and each having different name resolution servers.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=32)** For instance, we may have a VPN connection going through a public Wi-Fi network.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=36)** The DNS servers associated with these connections would come and go as the connections would.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=41)** If DNS resolution isn't handled dynamically per interface, then there may be old DNS entries that are irrelevant to our current environment.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=49)** For instance, if we disconnect from the VPN but the DNS entries reflect devices on those networks.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=54)** We choose which forms of name resolution we use and in which order in the /etc/nsswitch.conf file.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=61)** Let's take a look at that.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=63)** In a terminal typing cat space /etc/nsswitch.conf and hit enter.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=68)** If necessary, scroll until you see the host line.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=71)** This is the order in which your system resolves host names.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=74)** Mine says files DNS and then my host name.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=78)** This means it consults the local /nc/host file first and then ask DNS to resolve the name and lastly, the NSS - my host name plug-in module which provides host name resolution for the locally configured system host name as returned by the get host name system call.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=94)** If you wanted to include the systemd - resolvd service in the action, we'd add the word resolv to this line.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=100)** We'd have to decide where we want to include it to determine the order.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=104)** We can also move the /etc/resolv.conf file and then link the /run/systemd/resolve/resolv.conf file in its place.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=113)** You can also enable resolvd by configuring per interface systemd - networkd configuration files.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=119)** See another video in this course for more information.
>
> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-systemd-resolved?u=76281980&t=122)** To run the service, we need to start it and then enable it to make it persistent.

#### Use Nmap and netstat to audit network services
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-nmap-and-netstat-to-audit-network-services?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-nmap-and-netstat-to-audit-network-services?u=76281980&t=0)** - It's important when we're securing a system to turn off services that are not being used.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-nmap-and-netstat-to-audit-network-services?u=76281980&t=4)** If it's not running, it's difficult to exploit.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-nmap-and-netstat-to-audit-network-services?u=76281980&t=8)** To get the status of services on the Linux system that uses system d, you can type in sudo systemctl list-units

> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-nmap-and-netstat-to-audit-network-services?u=76281980&t=56)** To shut down a service, type sudo systemctl stop and then the service name.
