#### Set up limits on users
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=0)** - Linux has the ability to limit the amount of system resources available to user processes.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=5)** These limits include the number of open files.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=8)** The maximum size of user created files.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=10)** How much memory is used, and more.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=13)** We can change these limits using the ulimit command, but they revert back to defaults on the next reboot unless we keep them in the etc security [limits.com](https://limits.com) file.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=22)** Let's take a look at this file.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=23)** In a terminal, type in vi /etc/security/[limits.com](https://limits.com) and hit enter.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=30)** Now, scroll to the bottom.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=32)** In vi, you can go to the bottom by pressing the uppercase g when not in insert mode.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=37)** Here, we have some example entries.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=39)** We see that we have four columns.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=40)** Domain, Type, Item, and Value.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=45)** Domain can be a user, group, or wildcard.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=48)** Type can be one of two types, soft or hard.
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=51)** These are the two types of limits that we can have.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=53)** The soft limit is the one that we use to restrict users.
>
> **[0:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=56)** The hard limit has to be set by the admin user, and limits the maximum value for the soft limit.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=62)** For instance, if the admin set the hard limit of processes to 100,000 then the soft limit restricting users can never be higher than this.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=70)** Item is the thing we're restricting the limit of.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=72)** So, there's many different items.
>

>

>

>

>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=93)** There are more items that I didn't cover.

>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=102)** Now, go back to your terminal and press escape, colon, q and hit enter.
>
> **[1:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/set-up-limits-on-users?u=76281980&t=107)** You can check the current limits by using the ulimit command with a dash a option.

#### Determine which users have logged in
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Often an administrator needs to know who is logged in now or even who was logged in in the past.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=5)** Let's start by talking about finding out who is logged in right now.

>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=11)** Type in users into a terminal and hit Enter.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=14)** This will list the users logged into the system.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=16)** It doesn't tell very much information though.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=18)** The who command gives a bit more.

> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=20)** Type in who and hit Enter.

> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=26)** The next step is to go to the w command, perhaps the shortest command in Linux.

> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=30)** Type in w and hit Enter.

> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=33)** This method is my favorite.

> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=42)** Notice the header.

> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=48)** And the system load averages for the past one, five, and 15 minutes.

> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=53)** If you want to see when the users will logged into the system in the past, we use the last command.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=57)** Type in clear, then type in last and hit Enter.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=60)** This shows when the users logged in, and if you notice by scrolling up also shows who is logged in currently.

> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=68)** As a side point, if you want to see the system shutdowns, add -x.

> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/determine-which-users-have-logged-in?u=76281980&t=72)** Bring your line back, add space -x, and hit Enter.

#### Basic sudo configuration and usage
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Since the beginning of this course, we've been using Sudo to elevate privileges.
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=5)** We've also introduced the idea of using Su to switch to root, thus elevating privileges as well.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=12)** With Su, you have to type in the root user's password.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=15)** In order to do so, you have to know the root user's password.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=20)** This can be problematic because if we have multiple users that need to elevate to root, then they all need to know the root user's password.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=28)** If one leaves the company, then the root password has to be changed, and everyone needs to be updated.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=34)** Switching users keeps you logged in as root for the whole session, increasing the chances of human error.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=40)** With Sudo, you elevate privileges for one command only.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=45)** Also by switching to root, there's very little in the way of accountability.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=49)** All log messages say that the root user executed all commands.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=54)** With Sudo, all command execution is logged individually.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=59)** When using Su to switch to root, you are all powerful.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=63)** Sudo is more granular in that an administrator can decide which commands you are allowed to run, as well as which files you have access to.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=72)** Let's use Sudo to elevate our privileges.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=74)** In a terminal, type in Sudo space cat space slash etc slash shadow.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=81)** Sudo will then prompt us for our password, and execute cat with elevated privileges.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=87)** When cat is done, we'll return to our normal user role.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=90)** Hit enter, type in your password, and hit enter again.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=94)** Sudo allows us to elevate privileges for one command.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=98)** Sudo also caches the user's password for a short period of time for convenience.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=104)** Sudo has a configuration file that allows an administrator to set up who gets to elevate privileges, and what they get to do.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/basic-sudo-configuration-and-usage?u=76281980&t=111)** It's granular enough that we can even configure which command options the user can run.

#### Find suid/sgid files
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] SUID and SGID bits on executable files are special bits for privilege escalation.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=6)** The SUID bit allows non-user owners to execute commands with privileges of the user-owner.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=12)** In our terminal, let's do a long list on slash USR, slash BIN slash SU by typing in LS space dash L space slash USR slash BIN slash SU and hit enter.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=23)** The first thing we notice is that the file is bright red.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=26)** This tells us something special is happening.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=28)** Let's take a look at the permissions.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=30)** The permissions for the user-owner are RWS.
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=32)** Note that we can no longer see the user-owner's execute position.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=36)** The S in the user-owner's execute position means we have the SUID bit set.
>
> **[0:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=41)** If the S is lower case, then execute is set.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=45)** If the S is upper case, then execute permissions are not set.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=49)** The case of the letter S is the only way we can tell if the execute permissions are set.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=54)** When the SUID bit is set and a regular user, such as Bob, executes it, their privileges get elevated to that of the user-owners.
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=62)** In our example, Bob would be executing the SU command as root.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=66)** This means this command would have all of the power of the root user.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=70)** To se the SUID bit, we utilize an extra column of numeric permissions.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=74)** First, we decide what underlying permissions we want on the file.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=77)** In this case the file has RWX RX RX or seven five five permissions.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=84)** Then, we decide what extra bits we want.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=86)** In this case, SUID would just equal to four.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=90)** Setting these permissions via numeric mode would be chmod space four seven five five space slash USR slash BIN slash SU.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=98)** We can also use symbolic method as well by typing chmod space U plus S space slash USR slash BIN slash SU.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=106)** The SGID bit is very similar to SUID.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=110)** When we have the SGID bit set, the S resides in the group-owners execute position.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=115)** For instance, the slash USR slash BIN slash SCREEN command.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=119)** When a regular user executes a command with the SGID bit set, it runs with the privileges of the group owner of that file.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=126)** In this case, the screen group.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=129)** To add SGID bits to a file, we prefix the permissions with a two.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=133)** For the screen command, the permissions would be two seven five five.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=137)** So, we'd set SGID with chmod space two seven five five space slash USR slash BIN slash SCREEN.
>
> **[2:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=144)** We can also set this with symbolic mode in which we would use chmod space G plus S space slash USR slash BIN slash SCREEN.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=153)** Because these commands allow privilege escalation without prompting for a password, it may be advantageous to know where in the file system they are for security reasons.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=161)** We can use the find command to locate SUID and SGID files.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=165)** Type into a terminal SUDO space find space slash, that's where we're going to look, space dash type space F, we're going to search for files.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=175)** Space dash perm space dash 4000.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=179)** You can also type forward slash 4000 and it'll work Hit enter.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=184)** Type in your password if prompted.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=186)** This will find all files with an SUID bit set.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=189)** You can also use the symbolic method.
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=191)** Type in clear and then type in SUDO space find space slash space dash type, space F, space dash perm, space forward slash U equals S and hit enter.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=202)** To find files with the SGID bit set, we'll change the line slightly.
>
> **[3:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=207)** Type in clear and then type in SUDO space find space slash space dash type space F, space dash perm space dash 2000 and hit enter.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=217)** We can also use symbolic method here as well.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=219)** Type in clear.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=220)** Type in SUDO space find space slash space dash type, space F, dash perm, space, forward slash G equals S and hit enter.
>
> **[3:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=231)** This will list all files with the SGID bit set.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=234)** With symbolic method, we can find both.
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=236)** Bring your line back and add comma U equals S and hit enter.
>
> **[4:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=240)** This will list all files with SUID or SGID bit set.
>
> **[4:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=244)** Let me show you one last trick.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=245)** Type in clear and then bring your line back and add space dash exec space LS space dash L space dash dash color equals auto, space left curly bracket, right curly bracket space backslash semicolon and then hit enter.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/find-suid-sgid-files?u=76281980&t=264)** This executes the LS command and applies the dash dash color equals auto option to each file so we can see the permissions and the colors.

#### About TCP wrappers
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] Firewalls are incredibly important, they limit which packets get to which host; however there are times when you want more protection from within a network application.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=9)** If a network service is sending and receiving encrypted data, the firewall can't tell if it should allow it or deny it.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=15)** The services it self has access to the data , and can often make a better decision; one way of doing this is by using TCP Wrappers, note, that denying access with TCP Wrappers should be using conjunction with a firewall, and is not a replacement.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=29)** TCP Wrappers looks at two files, and determines whether to allow or deny access to the service.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=34)** This files are in /etc/hosts.allow, and /etc/hosts.deny.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=40)** A syntax of both of this files is the same .
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=43)** The services column is a coma separate list of services that this rule should apply to you.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=48)** The clients are coma separate list of hosts or IP addresses affected by the rule.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=52)** There are also accepted wild cards, which I'll get to in a moment.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=55)** The options columns are the action or actions that will be carried out such as allow or deny.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=61)** For matching the clients, we have some built in wild cards.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=65)** ALL, matches everything, and can be used for clients and services.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=69)** LOCAL, matches hosts without a period in their fully qualified dominium; such as local hosts.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=75)** KNOWN, indicates that the host name is known.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=77)** Be careful with this, as it may cause problems if your DNS service is not accessible.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=82)** UNKNOWN, is the exactly the opposite of known, and the same precaution apply.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=86)** Lastly, PARANOID causes a connection to be dropped, if forward and reverse DNS lookups don't match.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=92)** A guess the IP address using the host name, and then guess the host name using the IP.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=97)** This rules will go in either the allow, or deny file, there's a bit of difference between this two files.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=103)** the deny file can means lines of rules that deny access to services.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=106)** Since the default policy is allow, there's no need to allow access in this file, the allow file contains lines of rules that either, allow or deny access.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=116)** Both files can effectively allow and deny access to services they just do it differently.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=121)** One strategy is to deny all in the host that deny file, and specifically allow in the host that allow file.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=127)** This will be the firewall of doing things.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=130)** Another, would be to only use the deny file to deny specific access.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=135)** Lastly we could just use the allow file and specify allow and deny rules in the same file.
>
> **[2:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=140)** It's up to you, but beware that, if a rule and the host that allow file, allows connection then it's allowed.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=145)** If a rule and a host at deny file denies a connection, then it's denied, however; if a rule and the host that deny file, denies a connection, and there's also a rule and a host at allow file, that allows it, that connection is allowed.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=158)** Host that allow overrides host that deny.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=162)** You can only have one rule peer service in either file.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=164)** Order is important; if a connection is allowed at the top of the file, and denied at the bottom, it will never get to the bottom.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=171)** It matches the first instance.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=173)** If there are no rules matching a service in either file, then the service is granted.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=178)** Lastly, any changes to the files happen automatically; without restarting services.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=184)** Lets take a look at the deny file first.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=187)** In a terminal, typing: sudo vi /etc/ hosts.deny, and hit enter.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=194)** We see some comments, but no rules.
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=196)** But have one line denying everything.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=198)** Remember that the allow file overrides the deny file.
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=201)** Well deny everything in this file, and then grant access in the allow file.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=205)** Go in the insert mode by pressing the I key, and then clear to the bottom, type in ALL: ALL; this denies access to all services on all networks, now lets save that, by pressing scape, colon, x, s, exclamation mark, and hitting enter.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=223)** Now we'll move to the allow file.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=225)** Be aware that all new connections will be blocked, including those to the SSH service.
>
> **[3:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=229)** Something to be aware of if you're doing this to the network.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=233)** Now lets edit the allow file; typing sudo vi /etc/host.allow and hit enter.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=241)** Go to insert mode again, and go to the bottom of the file.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=245)** In this file we can grant access to whichever services we want.
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-tcp-wrappers?u=76281980&t=249)** Lets talk about some example rules.

#### Perform basic OpenSSH 2 client configuration
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=0)** - SSH has many options, including which ciphers to use, which host a client can connect to and how it operates.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=7)** However, usually a client configuration uses the defaults and is much easier to work with in the SSH server configuration.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=13)** There are three ways of passing configuration options to the SSH client.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=17)** They're processed in the following order: command line options, the per user configuration file stored in the user's home directory.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=24)** And lastly, the system wide configuration at /etc/ssh/ssh_config For each parameter, the first value will be used.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=34)** So any options specified on the command line override the configuration files.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=38)** Any option in the users configuration file overrides the system wide configuration file.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=43)** Let's start by looking at our system wide configuration file.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=46)** Later in this chapter, we'll talk about the per user configuration as well.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=49)** Let's load the system wide configuration file into less.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=52)** In a terminal type in less -Q /etc/ssh/ssh_config
>
> **[1:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=62)** and hit enter.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=64)** I've added the -Q option to quit the error bell in less.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=67)** Configuration options in these files are stored in key value pairs on individual lines.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=71)** Any line that starts with a hash symbol is a comment.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=74)** In the case of the SSH configuration items, it will be the name of the item and the default value.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=80)** To edit this setting will uncomment the line and change the value.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=85)** There are not very many uncommented lines.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=88)** The first one is the host line followed by the pattern of host a client is able to connect to.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=93)** In my case, it's an asterisk, meaning all hosts.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=96)** The next line that's uncommented is the GSS API authentication line.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=101)** The value is set to yes to use the generic security services API.
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=105)** This is an IETF standard for doing strong encrypted authentication in network based applications.
>
> **[1:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=110)** It's what we use to use Kerberos with SSH.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=114)** The next line that's uncommented, is the forward X11, trusted line.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=117)** This is set to yes.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=119)** If set to yes, the remote clients will have access to the full original display.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=124)** If set to no, the remote client will not have access to the entire display, and the security tokens will expire after 20 minutes.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=131)** After this time remote clients will be refused.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=134)** The last uncommented lines are locale related variables.
>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=138)** To see what settings we can modify, let's take a look at the man page for this file.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=141)** Press Q to quit.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=143)** Now type man SSH_config and hit enter.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=148)** This man page explains all possible options including ciphers, optimizations, and generic configuration items.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-openssh-2-client-configuration?u=76281980&t=156)** Keep this man page in mind when configuring the SSH client as it explains every option.

#### About OpenSSH 2 server host keys
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-openssh-2-server-host-keys?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-openssh-2-server-host-keys?u=76281980&t=0)** - There are multiple ways of authenticating using SSH, but the two most common are passwords and private public key pairs.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-openssh-2-server-host-keys?u=76281980&t=7)** For this exercise, we're going to need both of our VM's booted up, and you'll need the IP address of your RH host 2vm.

> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-openssh-2-server-host-keys?u=76281980&t=112)** When using SSH copy id, two things happen.

> **[2:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-openssh-2-server-host-keys?u=76281980&t=122)** And second, the fingerprint of the remote server is stored in the local user's known host file.

>
> **[2:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-openssh-2-server-host-keys?u=76281980&t=138)** This file stores the IP address of the remote servers as well as their matching fingerprints.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-openssh-2-server-host-keys?u=76281980&t=143)** If the remote server ever changes it's IP address, you'll need to delete it's lane from this file before connecting again.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/about-openssh-2-server-host-keys?u=76281980&t=149)** Don't worry about remembering this as the SSH client will refuse to connect and remind you to delete the line.

#### Perform basic GnuPG configuration and revocation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] GPG, or GNU Privacy Guard, is an implementation of the proprietary PGP, or Pretty Good Privacy program.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=7)** It's primarily used to encrypt emails but can be used to encrypt files as well.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=11)** GPG utilizes a combination of public and private keys, making it difficult to crack.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=16)** GPG should be installed but if it is not, you'll want to install the package named gnupg2.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=22)** We're going to start out by generating some GPG keys.

> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=93)** The public key we can distribute to other systems and users.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=97)** They then would encrypt files with it and send them back to us, only we can decrypt the files using our private key.

> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=105)** Type in clear and type in gpg2 --export user1 , and then the file name, I'll name it user1.pub.
>
> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=116)** If you want to append it to an email you'll need an ASCII text to do so, add the --armor option.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=123)** If you don't want to send a public key or add it to an email and text, then maybe you want to store it on a key server on the Internet.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=129)** If so type in gpg2 --send-keys and then, paste in your public identity.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=137)** And hit Enter, this will send the key to the public key server at [gnupg.net](https://gnupg.net).
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=143)** You can check the key out by pulling it back down again.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=145)** Type in gpg2 --keyserver hkp://keys.[gnupg.net](https://gnupg.net) --recv-keys ,
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=161)** and then paste in your public key identity again.
>
> **[2:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=164)** (mouse clicking) This should retrieve the key, if not hit it a second time and see what happens.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=171)** Once you've distributed the public key to someone they'll need to import it into their key ring.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=175)** To do so they'll use a --import option with the path.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=178)** If someone has stored the key in a current directory you could add it to the key ring by typing in gnupg2 --import the path to the key file.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=187)** Then, they can verify with the --list-keys option.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=190)** One last thing we might want to do is to make a revocation certificate so you can nullify your key pair in case you lose a private identity.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=197)** To create the certificate you'd type gpg2 --gen-revoke user1 enter a reason for the certificate an optional description, and a pass phrase to unlock the secret key for user1.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=210)** The certificate will be generated and displayed on screen.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/perform-basic-gnupg-configuration-and-revocation?u=76281980&t=213)** Copy and paste this into a separate file and store in a secure location for use later with gpg2 --edit-key user1, if needed.

#### Use GPG to encrypt, decrypt, sign, and verify files
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - We can encrypt and decrypt files with GPG for privacy.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=3)** We can also sign files as well, ensuring they haven't been tampered with.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=7)** Let's start by encrypting a file.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=8)** Let's encrypt the etc password file and save it as a new file.

> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=12)** To do so, in a terminal type in, gpg2, space, dash, dash, armor, space, dash, dash, recipient, space, user1, space, dash, dash, output, space, passwd.gpg, that'll be our encrypted file.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=30)** Space, dash, dash, encrypt, space, and then the file we want to encrypt is slash, etc, slash, P-A-S-S-W-D, and hit enter.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=39)** We didn't have to use the dash, dash, armor option which saves encrypted files as a text file, but this allows us to view it with cat, so let's do that.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=46)** Type in cat, space, passwd.gpg, we can see that it's encrypted.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=52)** Now if you want to decrypt this and display the output on the screen, type in clear and type in gpg2, space, dash, dash, decrypt, space, passwd.gpg.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=63)** If it prompts you for your password, type it in now.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=67)** If you want to decrypt it in place without displaying it on the screen, just type in clear, and then type in gpg2, space, passwd.gpg, and hit enter.
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=78)** This will restore the original file, and not just display the unencrypted contents.
>
> **[1:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=82)** We can also encrypt and sign a file, let's use the etc password file again.
>
> **[1:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=86)** Bring your encrypt line back, and add dash, dash, sign to it.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=90)** Say yes to override the file and hit enter again.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=93)** That's it, the decryption step is exactly the same.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/use-gpg-to-encrypt-decrypt-sign-and-verify-files?u=76281980&t=96)** When you decrypt it, GPG will display a message, telling you if the signature is good or not.

#### SSH port tunnels
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We've talked about using different protocols for remote GUI access.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=4)** If you want step-by-step instructions on how to install and configure programs for remote desktop on Linux, search for my desktops and remote access course in the course library.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=12)** In that course, I have separate videos for remote desktop protocols.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=16)** Now, let's talk about remote access using SSH, and some of the other features of that protocol.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=22)** SSH comes built in to every Linux distribution, although it might not be turned on by default.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=26)** SSH is a powerful and very underused tool.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=30)** SSH can provide remote login functionality.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=33)** SSH can execute commands remotely, even without an interactive login.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=39)** SSH has the ability to copy files across a network with the SCP command, and it has an interactive interface like FTP, but secure.
>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=47)** SSH can act as a generic encrypted tunnel that we can pipe data through, and also has several different port forwarding modes.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=53)** Local, remote and dynamic, or SOCKS proxy.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=57)** In this course, we'll focus on tunneling, as well as port forwarding.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=61)** There are several ways of sending data through an SSH tunnel, first, let's talk about piping it.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=66)** We can take the output of any command and pipe it into an SSH tunnel easily, although it's important to think about what happens to that data on the other end.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=74)** For instance, if I wanted to read a file, send it through a tunnel, then redirect that output back to the file system, I could.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=81)** In this case, the cat command reads the etc password file on the local machine, and then pipes it into the SSH tunnel we just created when we logged in.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=90)** The command string inside quotes is executed on the remote end, on the remote end, the cat + Space + hyphen + Space, greater than command reads from standard in and writes through standard out.
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=101)** In this example, we are essentially copying the file.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=104)** However, it can take standard out from any command, including binary data.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=108)** SSH also has the ability to redirect traffic through the tunnel by port forwarding.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=114)** Let's say that you have a remote database server with Mariadb running on it, Mariadb database runs on port 3306.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=120)** For security reasons, the firewall on the database host does not allow connections on port 3306 from the outside.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=127)** What we need to do is take local database client traffic, send it across the network using SSH, and then redirect that traffic to the database on the server.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=137)** The syntax is a bit odd, so let's go over it.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=139)** First, note that SSH will log in remotely as user one on rhhost2.[localnet.com](https://localnet.com), then it will take port number 3306 on our client machine and forward it through the tunnel and redirect it to 127.0.0.1:3306.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=156)** It's not obvious by the SSH command string, but this 127.0.0.1 is the local host after it's logged into the remote host.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=165)** Because it's redirecting data to 127.0.0.1:3306, the data will go to the servers running on rhhost2, that is answering on that port, in our case, Mariadb.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=177)** This allowed us to go through the open SSH port and redirect non-redirect SSH data through it to another service.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=184)** It is important to understand that 127.0.0.1 is processed on the remote host because we can change it to something else known to the remote host, for instance, if we changed 127.0.0.12, ubhost1.[localnet.com](https://localnet.com), and rhhost knew of this host, it would forward the traffic to it.
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=202)** In this sense, SSH is routing traffic inside the network for us.
>
> **[3:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=206)** We're not limited to one port either, they forward multiple ports, we just include multiple segments.
>
> **[3:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=212)** For instance, to forward both ports 3306 and 5901, we just duplicate the forwarding portion and change the ports.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=220)** SSH can do remote port forwarding as well.
>
> **[3:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=223)** This means instead of grabbing the local port and sending it remotely, it grabs the remote port and brings it back to the local host.
>
> **[3:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=230)** And it might be hard to imagine when we'd do this, so let me give you a scenario.
>
> **[3:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=234)** Let's say we have rhhost one and rhhost two again.
>
> **[3:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=238)** What we want to do is SSH from rhhost one directly to rhhost two, however, rhhost two is behind a firewall.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=245)** When we try to SSH, it blocks our incoming connection.
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=248)** This firewall, however, does not block outgoing SSH connections from rhhost two, so on rhhost two, we SSH out to a host accessible to both rhhost one and rhhost two.
>
> **[4:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=260)** In my example, it would be our ubhost one vm.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=263)** We then use remote port forwarding to grab a port that we know is not being used.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=268)** I usually choose a very high number port, such as 10,000, and we forward the traffic on that port back through the tunnel to rhhost two.
>
> **[4:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=276)** It's important to note that we leave this connection up.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=279)** Looking at the syntax in the diagram, we can see that the port of 10,000 is a remote port on ubhost one.
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=285)** Then we forward the traffic to 127.0.0.1:22.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=290)** That portion is processed by rhhost two as it's own local host address.
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=295)** This means it grabs the remote port 10,000 on ubhost one, and brings it back to rhhost two and delivers it to the local port 22.
>
> **[5:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=304)** Now back on rhhost one, we SSH to ubhost one on port 10,000, which we previously set up.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=311)** This open SSH connection from rhhost two grabs the traffic and brings it back to the tunnel, sneaking past the firewall.
>
> **[5:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=318)** I'd like to say right now that you should use this for good and not for evil.
>
> **[5:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=322)** In my example, we severed the firewall for a remote login session where we could have applied the database scenario that we did earlier.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/ssh-port-tunnels?u=76281980&t=329)** We could be using the database client accessing a public host that has forwarding traffic inside the public network.

#### Taking the exam
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The LPIC-1 version five certification is made up of two exams.
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=3)** For this exam, time management is key.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=6)** You have roughly 60 questions to accomplish in 90 minutes, but the minutes can go by fast.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=11)** The exam consists of multiple choice and fill in the blank questions.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=15)** Complete the easy questions first.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=17)** If you get hung up on one difficult item and run out of time before getting to the easier items, you may fail the test.
>
> **[0:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=23)** The test really does follow the exam objectives.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=26)** The Linux Professional Institute has told you what is on the exam, so pay close attention to the objectives.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=31)** Each LPI exam is ranked on a scale from 200 to 800 with a passing score of 500.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=37)** Since exam questions vary in difficulty, the number of correct answers required to reach the passing score of 500 will depend on the exact combination of questions on the exam you take.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=46)** For security purposes, candidates are not always given the same questions as one another for a particular exam.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=52)** The exam passing score is based on LPI psychometric studies to ensure all exams have a consistent level of difficulty.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=58)** The number of questions is also tied to the total of the weights for the objectives on the exam.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=63)** This exam has a total weight count of 60.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=66)** As such, the exam has 60 questions.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=69)** For each weighting, there will be one question.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=71)** For objective weightings, see the exam objectives.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=75)** As of this recording, the price of the exam was 200 US dollars and there are no prerequisites for taking the exam.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=81)** Keep in mind that the technologies and commands listed in the objectives in this course may not be exhaustive.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=87)** LPI reserves the right to change the test as they see fit.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=91)** Lastly, practice, practice, practice.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/lpic-1-exam-102-version-5-0-cert-prep/taking-the-exam?u=76281980&t=93)** Walk through this course more than once, complete the assessment questions more than once without using Google or your notes, and good luck.
